Rahn — Pledge
intermediate
8 min
The Definition and Legal Framework of Pledge (Rahn)
Defining الرهن and enumerating its ten aspects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and technical definition of rahn, identify its proof from the Quran, list its pillar and conditions, and explain the creditor's right of holding.

Core Idea
Rahn is a gratuitous contract that becomes binding only upon possession; the creditor holds the item as security, not as owner.

This passage opens the chapter on pledge (rahn) by establishing its fundamental structure. The commentator outlines ten aspects, beginning with the linguistic meaning and proof from the Quran, then detailing the pillar (offer), conditions (binding and validity), and the ruling (creditor's right of holding). Understanding these elements is essential before proceeding to detailed rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic meaning and its Quranic basis
Find the definition of rahn as 'confinement' (حبس) and note the Quranic verse (74:38) that supports this meaning. Understand that linguistically it does not imply transfer of ownership.
2
Locate the primary proof from the Quran
Extract the proof from Quran 2:283 (فَرِهَانٌ مَقْبُوضَةٌ). Recognize that the command to take pledges indicates the permissibility and recommended practice during debt transactions.
The proof is a command (أمر) indicating permissibility, not obligation.
3
Extract the pillar (rukn) and distinguish it from conditions
Identify the pillar as the offer (إيجاب) from the pledgor, such as 'I pledge this to you.' Note that acceptance is a condition, not a pillar, because pledge is a gratuitous contract.
Gratuitous contracts are completed by offer alone; acceptance is external.
4
Distinguish conditions of binding (luzum) and validity (jawaz)
The condition of binding is possession (قبض). The conditions of validity are: the item must be specifically divided and separated, free from others' rights, and capable of satisfying the debt. Without these, the contract is not valid.
Without possession, the pledge is not binding but may be revocable.
5
Articulate the ruling: the creditor's right of holding
The ruling is that the creditor has the right to hold the pledged item until the debt is paid. This is a right of possession, not ownership. The creditor does not own the item.
This ruling distinguishes rahn from a sale or transfer of ownership.

Source Text

كتاب الرهن"
(هو حبس الشيء بحق يمكن أخذه منه كالدين)

Book of Pledge: It is the holding of a thing by right from which it can be taken, like a debt. 1] His statement: Book of Pledge; the connection of the Book of Pledge to the Book of Hunting is that each of pledge and hunting is a means of acquiring wealth. The discussion of pledge occurs in topics: first, its linguistic meaning; second, its proof; third, its pillar; fourth, its condition of binding; fifth, its condition of validity; sixth, its ruling; seventh, its cause; eighth, its description; ninth, its meaning according to jurists; tenth, its merits. As for its linguistic meaning, it is confinement by any means. Allah said: 'Every soul is held in pledge for what it has earned'—i.e., confined for the sins it has earned. One says: rahantu the thing and irtahantu it; the plural is rihan and ruhun and rihan; and al-rahn al-marhun is named by the infinitive. Al-madayinah. And its proof is His statement: 'Then pledges taken in hand'—a command to take the pledge and its possession during the time of debt. And its pillar is the offer: the statement of the pledgor: 'I pledge this thing to you for the debt you have against me,' or 'take it'; and acceptance is a condition for it, because the pledge is a gratuitous contract, as it does not require anything by itself, and a gratuitous contract is completed by offer without acceptance, such that if one swears not to pledge and then pledges without the other accepting, he breaks his oath. And the condition of its binding is possession. And the condition of its validity is that it be divided, separated, free from the right of another, and that the pledge be such that satisfaction can be obtained from it like a debt, so that a pledge is not valid for what is not property, such as hudud, qisas, and manumission. And its ruling is that the creditor owns the pledge with respect to the right of holding, so that he has priority in holding it until (the debt is paid).
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on pledge (rahn) by defining it linguistically and technically. The commentator first connects this chapter to the previous one on hunting, noting that both are means of acquiring wealth. He then outlines ten topics to be covered: linguistic meaning, proof, pillar, condition of binding, condition of validity, ruling, cause, description, juristic meaning, and merits. The linguistic meaning of rahn is confinement, supported by Quran 74:38. The proof is from Quran 2:283, which commands taking pledges in hand during debt. The pillar (rukn) is the offer from the pledgor, such as 'I pledge this to you for your debt' or 'take it.' Acceptance is a condition but not a pillar because the contract is gratuitous (tabarru'), and gratuitous contracts are completed by offer alone—if one swears not to pledge and then pledges without acceptance, he breaks his oath. The condition of binding (luzum) is possession (qabd). The conditions of validity (jawaz) are: the pledged item must be specifically divided, separated, and free from others' rights; and it must be such that the debt can be recovered from it; thus a pledge is not valid for non-property rights like hudud, qisas, or manumission. The ruling (hukm) is that the creditor has the right to hold the pledge, i.e., he has priority in keeping it until the debt is paid.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A contract where a property is held as security for a debt, allowing the creditor to hold it until the debt is paid.
مرتهن (murtahin): The creditor who holds the pledge as security.
راهن (rahin): The debtor who gives the pledge as security.
قبض (qabd): Physical possession or delivery; a condition for the pledge to become binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for the pledge contract to become binding (lazim)?
A) Offer and acceptance
B) Possession (qabd)
C) Written contract
D) Witnesses

Key Takeaway
Rahn is a gratuitous security contract perfected by possession; the creditor holds the item as security, not as owner.

Rahn — Pledge
beginner

The Hanafi Definition and Framework of Rahn
Introductory commentary on the book of rahn covering definition, evidence, pillars, conditions, and benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of rahn, identify its pillar (offer) and conditions (possession, divisibility, recoverability), and distinguish between conditions of validity and binding.

Core Idea
Rahn is a gratuitous contract perfected by offer; possession makes it binding, and the pledged item must be separable, property of value.

This passage opens the chapter on rahn (pledge) by providing a comprehensive overview of its definition, evidence, structure, and benefits. Understanding these foundational elements is essential before studying detailed rulings. We will explore the linguistic meaning, the Quranic proof, the pillar (offer), the conditions that make the contract valid and binding, and the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the definition: 'detention of a thing due to a right from which it can be taken'.
Break down the definition into its components: what is detained (a thing), for what purpose (due to a right), and how it functions (the right can be recovered from it). Note that 'can be taken' excludes non-property rights like hudud.
This definition shapes the entire chapter.
2
Identify the Quranic evidence and its legal implication.
The verse 'farihanun maqbuda' (2:283) is cited as the primary dalil. The imperative form indicates permissibility and recommendation. Also note verse 74:38 supports the linguistic meaning of detention.
The evidence establishes the legitimacy of rahn, not obligation.
3
Distinguish between the pillar (rukn) and conditions (shurut).
The passage states that the pillar is the offer (ijab) alone. Acceptance is a condition for completion because rahn is a gratuitous contract. Then separate conditions of binding (possession) and conditions of validity (divisibility, separateness, freedom from others' rights, recoverability).
This distinction is crucial for understanding defective vs. void pledges.
4
Apply the conditions to a hypothetical scenario.
Consider a case: a pledgor offers to pledge a house that is jointly owned and not partitioned. Under Hanafi law, such a pledge is invalid because it lacks divisibility and separateness. Trace which condition is violated.
The condition of being 'muqaddaman mufarazan' is strictly applied.

The Book of Pledge: It is the detention of a thing due to a right from which it can be taken, like a debt. [1] The Book of Pledge (It is the detention of a thing due to a right from which it can be taken, like a debt). 1] His saying: The Book of Pledge; the suitability of the Book of Pledge to the Book of Hunting is in that each of the pledge and hunting is a means of acquiring wealth. The discussion of pledge occurs in places: first, its linguistic meaning; second, its evidence; third, its pillar; fourth, its condition of binding; fifth, its condition of validity; sixth, its ruling; seventh, its cause; eighth, its description; ninth, its meaning according to the jurists; tenth, its benefits. As for its linguistic meaning, it is detention by any means. Allah said: 'Every soul is held in pledge for what it has earned' (74:38) i.e., detained for the sins it earned. It is said: rahantu al-shay'a and irtahantuhu. The plural is rihan and ruhun and rihan. And al-rahn is the pledged item, named by the infinitive. (1) As for its evidence: His saying: 'Then a pledge with possession' (2:283), a command to take the pledge and take possession of it at the time of debt. As for its pillar: it is the offer: the rahin's saying: 'I pledge this thing to you for the debt you have against me,' or 'take it.' Acceptance is a condition for it because the pledge is a gratuitous contract; the pledge does not by itself require anything, and a gratuity is completed by offer without acceptance, such that if someone swore not to pledge and then pledged without the other accepting, he would break his oath. As for the condition of binding: it is possession. As for the condition of validity: that it be divided, separated, free from the right of another, and that the pledge be such that recovery is possible from it, like a debt, so that the pledge is not valid for what is not property, like hudud, qisas, and manumission. As for its ruling: the mortgagee owns the pledged item in terms of detention, so that he has priority in holding it until the time of paying the debt during life, and if the pledgor dies, he has priority over other creditors; he recovers his debt from it, and what remains is for the creditors. As for its cause: the need for it, because a person may not find someone who will lend to him for free without a pledge, or bear with him without a pledge. As for its description: Most scholars say that the pledge is a guarantee upon the mortgagee, as will come. As for its legal interpretation: the matn author will discuss it. As for its benefits: it relieves the pledgor from the hardship of asking, and gives peace of mind to the mortgagee regarding the recovery of his money. If he stipulates that if it is lost without anything and the pledgor permits, the pledge is valid and the condition is void, because it is a change to a contract established by a lawful rule, and changing what is lawful is not permitted; and what is taken under a defective pledge is guaranteed. (1) Ibn Sama'a narrated from Abu Yusuf: if someone pledges half a house and delivers the house to the mortgagee, and it perishes, nothing of the debt is lost. Similarly, it is mentioned in Nawadir Hisham from Muhammad that in a defective pledge, its loss does not cause the debt to be lost. In al-Jami' al-Kabir: if a Muslim buys wine and pledges a pledge for its price, and the pledge is lost with him, he does not guarantee it, because it is a void pledge in the first instance, and it is concluded defectively. And Allah knows best. This is the summary of what is in Takmilat al-Bahr. [1] His saying: 'can be taken from it' is a precaution against pledging wine, and against pledging for hudud, qisas, and manumission. [2] His saying: 'and it is not possible to recover...' so pledging is not valid for a non-guaranteed object, like deposits and loans, nor for an object guaranteed by something else, like goods in the seller's hand or a pledge in the mortgagee's hand, contrary to objects guaranteed in themselves, like usurped property, dower, or compensation for khul' or blood money.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the chapter on Rahn (pledge) from the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah. The author begins by explaining the definition of rahn as stated in the matn: 'It is the detention of a thing due to a right from which it can be taken, like a debt.' He then outlines ten aspects of the discussion: linguistic meaning, evidence, pillar, condition of binding, condition of validity, ruling, cause, description, legal meaning according to jurists, and benefits. Linguistically, rahn refers to detention or confinement, supported by Quran 74:38. The evidence is from Quran 2:283, which commands taking a pledge with possession at the time of debt. The pillar (rukun) is the offer (ijab) from the pledgor, while acceptance (qabul) is a condition because rahn is a gratuitous contract (tabarru') which is perfected by offer alone. The condition of binding (shart al-luzum) is possession (qabd) by the mortgagee. The condition of validity (shart al-jawaaz) requires the pledged item to be divided, separate, free from others' rights, and such that the debt can be recovered from it—thus a pledge for non-property like hudud, qisas, or manumission is invalid. The ruling is that the mortgagee has the right to detain the item and priority over other creditors upon the pledgor's death. The cause is the need for security. The description: most scholars hold the pledge as a guarantee (mamun) upon the mortgagee. The legal interpretation is left to the matn. The benefits include relieving the pledgor from asking and reassuring the mortgagee. The author then discusses a conditional pledge and defective pledge rulings, including narrations from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Finally, he specifies that the definition excludes non-property and non-guaranteed objects.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A contract by which a thing is detained as security for a debt that can be recovered from it.
حبس (habs): Detention or confinement of an item as security.
مرتهن (murtahin): The mortgagee or the person who receives the pledge as security.
راهن (rahin): The pledgor or the person who gives the pledge.
قبض (qabd): Possession or taking physical control of the pledged item; a condition for the binding nature of the pledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the condition for a pledge to be binding (lazim) according to the passage?
A) The offer (ijab) from the pledgor
B) The acceptance (qabul) from the mortgagee
C) Possession (qabd) of the pledged item
D) The pledged item being divisible

Key Takeaway
Rahn is a permissible, gratuitous contract perfected by offer, made binding by possession, requiring the pledged item to be property that is separable, free from others' rights, and recoverable for the debt.

Rahn — Pledge
intermediate
10 min
Rahn: Non-Binding Until Delivery (Qabd)
Commentary establishing rahn as non-binding contract requiring delivery for binding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the binding nature of rahn and explain why delivery is a condition of binding, using the Quranic proof and the nature of rahn as a gratuitous contract.

Core Idea
A pledge becomes binding only when the pledgee takes possession — until then, the pledgor may withdraw.

The passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah establishes that the contract of pledge (rahn) is non-binding until delivery (qabd). It categorizes rahn as a gratuitous contract (tabarru'), analogous to a gift, where the promise is not binding until the object is handed over. The author presents the proof from the Quran (2:283) and refutes the Maliki position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the contract of rahn? It is a pledge where property is given as security for a debt. Ruling: It is valid (sahih) upon offer and acceptance but non-binding (ghayr lazim) until delivery.
2
Understand the Hanafi classification
Rahn is a gratuitous contract (tabarru') because the pledgor receives no direct benefit; this affects the binding rule.
3
Examine the Quranic proof
The verse 'فَرِهَنٌ مَقْبُوضَةٌ' describes the pledge as 'in hand'. According to Hanafi usul, an indefinite noun when described generalizes, indicating that every legitimate pledge must be possessed.
See dalil_block for full analysis.
4
Analyze the inter-madhhab dispute
Imam Malik argues rahn is binding immediately like sale because it involves property from both sides. The Hanafi response distinguishes between sale (compensation from both sides) and rahn (gratuitous from pledgor's side).
See madhhab_dispute_map.
5
Identify the conditions for a binding delivery
The pawned item must be mahuz (divided, not shared), mufarragh (free from pledgor's other rights), and mutamayyiz (distinct from other property). If these are met, delivery makes the contract binding.
These conditions ensure the pledge is clearly identified and surrendered.
6
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Before delivery, the pledgor can withdraw; after delivery, the contract is binding.

Source Text

كتاب الرهن / تعريفه وشروطه وما يصح به والتصرف فيه عنه بإيجاب وقبول غير لازم ، فللراهن تسليمه والرجوع عن بإيجاب وقبول غير لازم) : أي ينعقد حال كونه غير لازم، (فللراهن تسليمه والرجوع عنه) (۱) عمد، فإن الرهن يصح بها كما يصح الدين؛ لأنّ مالها إلى الدين، كما يفهم من قول «الهداية». (۲) ويمكن أن يقال ... إلى آخره، فيجوز أن يكون إيراد لفظ الدين على سبيل التمثيل دون الحصر، واختيار لفظ الحق على الدين لا يخلو عن الإشارة إلى هذا التعميم، قاله الجلبي [1] قوله : بإيجاب وقبول ... الخ ؛ الإيجاب هو قولُ الراهن : رهنتك هذا المال بدين لك عليّ، وما أشبه ذلك، والقبول هو قول المرتهن : قبلت. قال شيخ الإسلام خُواهَر زَادَه : الركنُ الإيجاب بمجرده ؛ لأنه عقد تبرع ، وكل ما هو كذلك يتم بالتبرع ، فإنَّ الرهن يتم بالمتبرع، أما المقدمة الأولى؛ فلأنَّ الراهن لا يستوجب بإزاء ما أثبت للمرتهن من اليد شيئاً، ولا نعني بالتبرع إلا هذا، وأما الثانية : أي إنّ ما هو كذلك يتم بالمتبرع ، وكالهبة والصدقة وغيرهما، والقبض شرط اللزوم. وقال مالك : يلزمُ بنفس العقد ؛ لأنه يختص بالمال من الجانبين، فصار كالبيع ؛ ولأنه عقد وثيقة فأشبه الكفالة. ولنا: قوله : فَرِهَنٌ مقبوضةٌ (۳) ، وصف الرهن بكونها مقبوضة، والنكرة إذا وصفت عمت كقوله : والله لا أكلم إلا رجلاً كوفياً. فيقتضي أن يكون كل الرهن مشروعاً لهذه الصفة، ولأنه عقد تبرع، لما أنّ الراهن لا يستوجب بمقابلته على المرتهن شيئاً ؛ ولهذا يجبر عليه، فلا بد من إمضائه كما في الوصيّة، وذلك بالقبض، هذا ما في شروح «الهداية». (۱) أي قبض القبض، لأن القبض شرط لزوم . ينظر: «الهداية»(٤ : ١٢٦). (۲) في ذخيرة العقبى»(ص٥٩٣). (۳) البقرة : ۲۸۳ . فإذا سلَّمَ فقبض محوزاً مفرغاً متميزاً لَزِمَ فقبض حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية أي تسليم الرهن بمعنى المرهون ، والرجوع عن الرهن بمعنى العقد، فإذا سلم (۱) محوز (۱۳۸۴) : أي مقسوماً غير شائع، (مفرغا) : أي غير مشغول بحق الراهن، حتى لا يجوز رهن الأرض بدون النخل، والشجر بدون الثمر، ودار فيها متاعُ الراهن بدون المتاع متميزاً لَزَمَ : أي إن كان متصلاً بحق الراهن خلقةً [١] قوله : بمعنى المرهون ؛ لا يخلو عن الإشارة إلى ضمير تسليمه مـن قبـيـل الاستخدام. (۲) قوله : محوزاً ؛ أي مقسوماً، كأنه اعتبر فيه معنى قولهم : تحاوز الفريقان في الحرب ؛ أي انعزل كل فريق من الآخر، كما يفهم من لفظ الجَوْهَرِي، وأما من جعله احترازاً عن المفترق كالزاهدي والأكمل، ومثله بالثمار على رؤوس الأشجار، فقد أخذه من الحوز ، بمعنى الجمع، كما هو المشهور. قاله الجلبي (٢). (۳) قوله : محوزاً ؛ قال في «الكفاية» (۳) ؛ أي مقسوماً، وهو احتراز عن رهن المشاع ، فإنّه لا يجوز عندنا ، وقوله : مفرغاً ؛ أي عن ملك الراهن، وهو احتراز عن رهن دارِ فيها متاعُ الراهن، وقوله : متميز ؛ أي لم يكن الرهن متصلاً بغيره اتصال خلقة، كما لو رهن الثمر على رأس الشجر دون الشجر ، لأنّ المرهون متصل بغير المرهون خلقة، فصار كالشائع. [٤]قوله : لزم ؛ أي العقد ؛ لوجود القبض بكماله ، قال في «الهداية»: وما لم يقبضه فالراهن بالخيار إن شاء سلّمه، وإن شاء رجعَ عن الرهن، لما ذكرنا أن اللزوم (٤) بالقبض، إذ المقصود لا يحصل قبله. (1) أي بإذن الراهن صريحاً أو ما جرى مجراه في المجلس وبعده بنفسه أو بنائبه كأب ووصي وعدل. ينظر : رد المحتار (۵ : ۳۰۸) (۲) في ذخيرة العقبى» (ص٥٩٤). (۳) (الكفاية» (۸ : ٦٩). (٤) ((الهداية (٤) : ١٢٦).

So the pledgor has the option to deliver it and to withdraw from it. (By offer and acceptance, it is non-binding): i.e., it is concluded while being non-binding. (So the pledgor has the option to deliver it and to withdraw from it) (1) deliberately, for the pledge is valid with them as the debt is valid; because its reference is to debt, as understood from the saying of 'al-Hidayah'. (2) And it can be said... etc., so it is permissible that the word 'debt' is mentioned as an example without restriction, and choosing the word 'right' over 'debt' is not devoid of indication to this generalization, said al-Jalabi. [1] His saying: by offer and acceptance... etc.; the offer is the pledgor's saying: I pledge this property to you for a debt due to me, and the like, and the acceptance is the pledgee's saying: I accept. Shaykh al-Islam Khwahar Zadeh said: The pillar is the offer alone, because it is a gratuitous contract, and everything that is like that is completed by the donor's act alone; for the pledge is completed by the donor. As for the first premise: because the pledgor does not acquire in return for what he establishes for the pledgee of possession anything, and we do not mean by a gratuitous contract except this. And as for the second: i.e., everything that is like that is completed by the donor's act alone, like gift and charity and others, and delivery is a condition of binding. Malik said: It becomes binding by the contract itself, because it pertains to property from both sides, so it becomes like sale; and because it is a security contract, so it resembles surety. And our proof: His saying (Quran): 'a pledged object in hand,' describing the pledge as being in hand, and an indefinite when described generalizes, like His saying: 'By Allah I will not speak except to a Kufan man.' So it requires that every pledge is legislated with this attribute. And because it is a gratuitous contract, since the pledgor does not acquire in return anything from the pledgee; therefore he is compelled to it, so its execution is necessary like a will, and that is by delivery. This is what is in the commentaries of 'al-Hidayah'. (1) i.e., the delivery of the object, because delivery is a condition of binding. See: al-Hidayah (4:126). (2) In Dhakhrirat al-Uqba (p. 593). (3) al-Baqarah: 283. Then when he delivers, then with possession of something that is possessed (divided), emptied, distinct, it becomes binding. 'Possessed' (in the text) is from 'al-Hasan al-Dirayah li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqayah': i.e., the delivery of the pledge meaning the pledged object, and withdrawal from the pledge meaning the contract. So when he delivers (1) something that is possessed (1384): i.e., divided, not shared; (emptied): i.e., not occupied by the pledgor's right, so that pawning land without palm trees, and trees without fruit, and a house containing the pledgor's belongings without the belongings is not permissible; (distinct): it becomes binding, i.e., if it is connected to the pledgor's right by creation? [1] His saying: meaning the pledged object; it is not devoid of indication to the pronoun of 'his delivery' from the aspect of usage. (2) His saying: 'possessed' (mahuzan): i.e., divided, as if he considered in it the meaning of their saying: the two parties isolated in war; i.e., each party separated from the other, as understood from al-Jawhari's wording. As for the one who made it a precaution against the separated like al-Zahidi and al-Akmal, and gave the example of fruits on top of trees, he took it from 'al-hawz' meaning collection, as is well-known. Said by al-Jalabi (2). (3) His saying: 'possessed'; he said in 'al-Kifayah' (3): i.e., divided, and it is a precaution against pawning undivided property, for it is not permissible with us. And his saying: 'emptied': i.e., from the pledgor's ownership, and it is a precaution against pawning a house containing the pledgor's belongings. And his saying: 'distinct': i.e., the pawned item is not connected to something else by creation connection, as if he pawns the fruit on top of the tree without the tree, because the pawned item is connected to what is not pawned by creation, so it becomes like undivided property. [4] His saying: 'it becomes binding'; i.e., the contract, due to the existence of complete delivery. Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah explains the fundamental ruling of the contract of rahn (pledge/pawn) in the Hanafi school. The passage type is a ruling with proofs, including a debate with Imam Malik. The author establishes that the contract of rahn is concluded (yan'aqid) by offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul) but it is not binding (ghayr lazim) upon conclusion. It only becomes binding (lazim) after the pledgee takes possession (qabd) of the pawned item. This is because rahn is classified as a gratuitous contract (tabarru')—the pledgor does not receive a direct material benefit from the pledgee for granting the right of possession. Therefore, like other gratuitous contracts such as gifts (hiba) and charity (sadaqa), the contract is not binding until the object is delivered. The passage presents a key intra-school opinion from Khwahar Zadeh, who argues that the only pillar (rukn) of the contract is the offer, and acceptance is not required; however, the relied-upon position is that both offer and acceptance are necessary for the contract to be concluded. The passage also refutes Imam Malik's view that rahn becomes binding immediately upon the contract, using two proofs: first, the Qur'anic verse 'فَرِهَنٌ مَقْبُوضَةٌ' (a pledged object in hand) which, according to Hanafi principles of interpretation, indicates that being in hand (possession) is an essential attribute of rahn. Second, analogy to gratuitous contracts which require delivery for binding effect. The passage then details the conditions of a valid delivery that leads to binding: the pawned object must be mahuz (divided, not shared/undivided), mufarragh (free from the pledgor's other rights—e.g., an empty house, land without trees if the trees are not included), and mutamayyiz (distinct from other objects—e.g., fruit on the tree must be separated or pawned with the tree). If these conditions are met and the pledgee takes possession (with the pledgor's permission, explicit or implied), the contract becomes binding. Until that point, the pledgor retains the right to withdraw from the contract. This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A contract in which a property is given as security for a debt, but the ownership remains with the pledgor.
إيجاب (ijab): The offer made by the pledgor, e.g., 'I pledge this property to you for your debt.'
قبض (qabd): The taking of possession by the pledgee, which is a condition for the contract to become binding.
تبرع (tabarru'): A gratuitous transaction where one party confers a benefit without receiving direct compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a contract of rahn become binding (lazim)?
A) Immediately upon offer and acceptance.
B) Only after the pledgee takes possession (qabd) of the pawned item.
C) Upon registration with a judge.
D) After a specific period of time has passed.

Key Takeaway
A pledge (rahn) is not binding until the pledgee physically takes possession of the pawned item, because rahn is a gratuitous contract and the Quran describes a pledge as being 'in hand'.

Rahn — Pledge
intermediate
8 min
Conditions for Binding Pledge and Meaning of Qabd
Explains conditions for binding pledge and the meaning of qabd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for a pledge to become binding (mahuz, mufargh, mutamayyiz) and explain why takhliyah constitutes qabd in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A pledge binds only when the pledgee takes possession of an item that is partitioned, vacant, and distinct.

This lesson examines the conditions that transform a pledge contract from a mere promise into a binding obligation. The Hanafi school meticulously defines what constitutes valid possession (qabd) and the qualities the pledged item must have. We will unpack the three key conditions—mahuz, mufargh, mutamayyiz—and explore the intra-madhhab debate on whether relinquishing control (takhliyah) suffices as possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
List the three conditions: mahuz (partitioned), mufargh (vacant), mutamayyiz (distinct). Understand their definitions as given in the text.
The text defines each term with examples.
2
Understand the purpose of each condition
Mahuz prevents ambiguity (no undivided share). Mufargh prevents conflict of interest (pledgor's other property not included). Mutamayyiz prevents physical inextricability (items attached by creation).
Each condition addresses a specific risk.
3
Define qabd and its types
Qabd is possession. It can be actual physical taking or takhliyah (relinquishing control so the pledgee can take). Understand that takhliyah is sufficient in the relied-upon position.
The text states: 'التخلية قبض فيه كما في البيع'.
4
Analyze the dispute on takhliyah
Contrast the Zahir al-Riwayah (takhliyah suffices for all) with Abu Yusuf (requires physical transfer for movables) and Malik (no qabd needed).
Identify the mufta bihi and the practical difference.

فإذا سلَّمَ فقبض محوزاً مفرغاً متميزاً لَزِمَ
أي تسليم الرهن بمعنى المرهون ، والرجوع عن الرهن بمعنى العقد، فإذا سلم (۱) محوز (۱۳۸۴) : أي مقسوماً غير شائع، (مفرغا) : أي غير مشغول بحق الراهن، حتى لا يجوز رهن الأرض بدون النخل، والشجر بدون الثمر، ودار فيها متاعُ الراهن بدون المتاع متميزاً لَزَمَ : أي إن كان متصلاً بحق الراهن خلقةً [١] قوله : بمعنى المرهون ؛ لا يخلو عن الإشارة إلى ضمير تسليمه مـن قبـيـل الاستخدام. (۲) قوله : محوزاً ؛ أي مقسوماً، كأنه اعتبر فيه معنى قولهم : تحاوز الفريقان في الحرب ؛ أي انعزل كل فريق من الآخر، كما يفهم من لفظ الجَوْهَرِي، وأما من جعله احترازاً عن المفترق كالزاهدي والأكمل، ومثله بالثمار على رؤوس الأشجار، فقد أخذه من الحوز ، بمعنى الجمع، كما هو المشهور. قاله الجلبي (٢). (۳) قوله : محوزاً ؛ قال في «الكفاية» (۳) ؛ أي مقسوماً، وهو احتراز عن رهن المشاع ، فإنّه لا يجوز عندنا ، وقوله : مفرغاً ؛ أي عن ملك الراهن، وهو احتراز عن رهن دارِ فيها متاعُ الراهن، وقوله : متميز ؛ أي لم يكن الرهن متصلاً بغيره اتصال خلقة، كما لو رهن الثمر على رأس الشجر دون الشجر ، لأنّ المرهون متصل بغير المرهون خلقة، فصار كالشائع. [٤]قوله : لزم ؛ أي العقد ؛ لوجود القبض بكماله ، قال في «الهداية»: وما لم يقبضه فالراهن بالخيار إن شاء سلّمه، وإن شاء رجعَ عن الرهن، لما ذكرنا أن اللزوم بالقبض، إذ المقصود لا يحصل قبله. (1) أي بإذن الراهن صريحاً أو ما جرى مجراه في المجلس وبعده بنفسه أو بنائبه كأب ووصي وعدل. ينظر : رد المحتار (۵ : ۳۰۸) (۲) في ذخيرة العقبى» (ص٥٩٤). (۳) (الكفاية» (۸ : ٦٩). (٤) ((الهداية (٤) : ١٢٦).
والتخلية قبض فيه كما في البيع، وضمن بأقل من قيمته ومن الدين كالثمر على الشجر يجب أن يميز ويفصل عنه، فالمفرغ يتعلق بالمحل، فيجب فراغه عما حلّ فيه كالثمر، وهو ليس بمرهون سواء كان اتصاله به خلقة أو مجاورة، والمتميز يتعلق بالحال في المحلّ، فيجب انفصاله عن محل غير مرهون إذا كان اتصالُهُ به خلقة، حتى لو كان اتصاله بالمجاورة لا يضرّ كرهن المتاع الذي في بيت الراهن. (والتخلية قبض فيه كما في البيع، التخلية أن يضعه الراهن في موضع يتمكن المرتهن من أخذه، هذا في ظاهر الرواية، وعن أبي يوسف ه لا يثبت في المنقول إلا بالنقل ؛ لأنه قبض موجب للضمان بمنزلة الغصب ، وعند مالك يلزم بدون القبض. (1) وضمن بأقل من قيمته ومن الدِّين، اعلم أنَّ هذا تركيب مشكل غفل قال في «الكفاية» (۲) : لأن الرهن استيفاء الدين حكماً ، والاستيفاء حقيقة لا يكون بدون القبض، فكذا الاستيفاء حكماً ؛ ولأن المقصود إضجار الراهن ؛ ليتسارع إلى قضاء الدين، وإنما يحصل هذا المقصود بدوام يد المرتهن عليه، وذلك إنما يكون بالقبض. [ ] قوله : وضَمِنَ ؛ شروع لبيان كيفية الضمان، وكميته بعد بيان دخوله تحت الضمان بقوله : فإذا سلّم وقبض...الخ ، قال في «الهداية»(۳) : فإذا سلم إليه وقبضه دخلَ في ضمانه، قاله الجلبي [١] قوله : مشكل ؛ وقال خواهر زاده : هذا خطأ ، واعتبر هذا بقول الرجل : مررت بأعلم من زيد وعمر، ويكون الأعلم غيرهما، ولو قال: بالأعلم من زيد وعمرو، يكون الأعلمُ واحداً منهما، وكلمة : من ؛ للتميز. كذا في «مشكلات القُدُوري». قاله الجلبي (ه). (1) ينظر: الشرح الصغير، وحاشية الصاوي عليه(۳: ۳۱۳)، وغيرهما. (۲) «الكفاية» (۸: ۷۰). (۳) «الهداية» (٤ : ١٢٧). (٤) في ذخيرة العقبى»(ص٥٩٤). (٥) في «ذخيرة العقبى»(ص٥٩٤).
When he hands over and takes possession of it being partitioned, vacant, and distinct, it becomes binding. That is, the delivery of the pledge meaning the pledged item, and the withdrawal from the pledge meaning the contract. So when he hands over (1) partitioned (1384): i.e., divided, not shared; (vacant): i.e., not occupied by the pledgor's right, such that it is not permissible to pledge land without palm trees, or trees without fruit, or a house containing the pledgor's goods without the goods; distinct: i.e., if it is connected to the pledgor's right by creation. [1] His statement: 'meaning the pledged item' is not free from hinting at the pronoun of its delivery by way of usage. (2) His statement: 'partitioned' i.e., divided, as if he considered in it the meaning of their saying: 'the two parties separated in war' i.e., each party isolated from the other, as understood from the wording of al-Jawhari. As for those who made it a precaution against the scattered like al-Zahidi and al-Akmal, and exemplified it with fruits on top of trees, they took it from 'al-hawz' meaning gathering, as is well-known. Al-Jalabi said it. (3) His statement: 'partitioned'; said in al-Kifayah (3): i.e., divided, and it is a precaution against pledging an undivided share (musha'), for it is not permissible according to us. His statement: 'vacant'; i.e., from the ownership of the pledgor, and it is a precaution against pledging a house in which there is the pledgor's goods. His statement: 'distinct'; i.e., the pledge is not connected to something else by creation, as if he pledges fruit on top of the tree without the tree, because the pledged item is connected to a non-pledged item by creation, so it becomes like an undivided share. [4] His statement: 'binding'; i.e., the contract, due to the existence of complete possession. He said in al-Hidayah: As long as he has not taken possession, the pledgor has the option: if he wishes he hands it over, if he wishes he withdraws from the pledge, because we mentioned that binding is by possession, for the purpose is not achieved before it. (1) i.e., with the express permission of the pledgor or what takes its place in the session and after it, by himself or by his deputy like a father, executor, or trustee. See: Radd al-Muhtar (5:308). (2) From Dhakhirat al-Uqba (p.594). (3) Al-Kifayah (8:69). (4) Al-Hidayah (4:126). And relinquishing (takhliyah) is possession therein as in sale, and he is liable for the lesser of its value and the debt. Like fruit on the tree must be distinguished and separated from it, for the vacant relates to the locus, so it must be empty of what is in it like fruit, and it is not pledged whether its connection is by creation or adjacency. The distinct relates to the state in the locus, so it must be separated from a non-pledged locus if its connection to it is by creation, but if its connection is by adjacency it does not harm, like pledging the goods that are in the pledgor's house. (And relinquishing is possession therein as in sale: relinquishing is that the pledgor places it in a place where the pledgee can take it; this is in the apparent narration. And from Abu Yusuf: it is not established for movables except by physical transfer, because it is possession that necessitates liability akin to usurpation. And according to Malik, it becomes binding without possession. (1) And he is liable for the lesser of its value and the debt. Know that this is a problematic construction that [؟] neglected. He said in al-Kifayah (2): Because the pledge is a virtual recovery of the debt, and actual recovery cannot be without possession, so likewise virtual recovery. And because the purpose is to pressure the pledgor to hasten to repay the debt, and this purpose is achieved only by the pledgee's continuous hand over it, which is only through possession. [ ] His statement: 'and he is liable'; is a commencement to explain the manner of liability and its amount after explaining its entry under liability by his statement: 'when he hands over and takes possession...' etc. He said in al-Hidayah (3): When he hands it over and takes possession, it enters into his liability, said al-Jalabi. [1] His statement: 'problematic'; Khaward Zadeh said: This is an error, and consider this by the saying of a man: 'I passed by the most learned of Zaid and Amr', where the most learned is someone other than them; and if he said: 'by the most learned from Zaid and Amr', the most learned is one of them. And the word 'min' is for distinction. Thus in Mushkilat al-Quduri, said al-Jalabi (5). (1) See: al-Sharh al-Saghir and Hashiyat al-Sawi upon it (3:313) and others. (2) Al-Kifayah (8:70). (3) Al-Hidayah (4:127). (4) Dhakhirat al-Uqba (p.594). (5) Dhakhirat al-Uqba (p.594).
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on Al-Hidayah explaining the conditions for a pledge (rahn) to become binding (lazim). The core ruling is that when the pledgor hands over the pledged item and the pledgee takes possession (qabd) of it, the contract becomes binding. However, the qabd must meet three conditions: the item must be (1) mahuz (partitioned, i.e., not undivided share), (2) mufargh (vacant, i.e., free from the pledgor's rights or property that is not pledged), and (3) mutamayyiz (distinct, i.e., not physically connected to non-pledged property by creation). The commentary elaborates each term. For 'mahuz', it means divided and not shared; pledging an undivided share (musha') is invalid. For 'mufargh', it means empty of the pledgor's ownership; e.g., one cannot pledge land without the palm trees on it, or a house containing the pledgor's furniture without including the furniture. For 'mutamayyiz', if the pledged item is naturally connected to something not pledged (like fruit on a tree), it must be separated; otherwise it is like an undivided share. Once proper qabd is achieved, the contract binds, and the pledgor can no longer withdraw. The commentary then discusses takhliyah (relinquishing control) as a valid form of qabd by analogy to sale. According to the apparent position (Zahir al-Riwayah), if the pledgor places the item where the pledgee can take it, qabd is achieved. However, Imam Abu Yusuf holds that for movable property (manqul), actual physical transfer is required because possession that entails liability must be definitive like usurpation. Imam Malik holds that no qabd is needed for the contract to bind. The passage also addresses the liability (daman) of the pledgee after qabd: he is liable for the lesser of the item's value and the debt. The final part discusses a linguistic nuance in the phrasing 'lesser of its value and the debt', noting a potential confusion that is clarified by examples from Arabic grammar.

Key Terms
محوز (mahūz): Partitioned/divided; not an undivided share (musha'). The pledged item must be separated from other property to avoid ambiguity.
مفرغ (mufargh): Vacant; the pledged item must be empty of the pledgor's other rights or property that is not pledged.
متميز (mutamayyiz): Distinct; the pledged item must not be physically connected by creation to something not pledged; if it is, it must be separated.
قبض (qabḍ): Possession/taking delivery; the act that makes the pledge binding. Can be actual physical taking or takhliyah (relinquishing).
تخلية (takhliyah): Relinquishing control; the pledgor leaves the item in a place where the pledgee can take it without obstruction.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability; after qabd, the pledgee is liable for the pledged item. He must compensate if it is lost, up to the lesser of its value or the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the term 'محوز' (mahuz) mean in the context of a valid pledge (rahn)?
A) The pledged item must be valuable and lawful.
B) The pledged item must be partitioned and not an undivided share.
C) The pledged item must be vacant of the pledgor's other property.
D) The pledged item must be distinct from any other property.

Key Takeaway
A pledge is binding only when the pledgee takes possession of an item that is mahuz, mufargh, and mutamayyiz, and takhliyah qualifies as possession in the relied-upon Hanafi view.

Takhliya as Qabd in Pawn and the Daman Principle
Explains the ruling on delivery and liability of a pawn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two methods of qabd in rahn (takhliya and naql), identify the ruling on liability when the pawn perishes, and apply the daman principle (lesser of value and debt).

Core Idea
Takhliya suffices as possession in pawn, and the pawnee's liability is capped at the lesser of the pawn's value and the debt.

Possession (qabd) is a key element that makes a pawn contract binding. This lesson explains that in the Hanafi school, the pawner can simply enable the pawnee to take the item (takhliya), without physical handover, to establish liability. However, there is an internal dispute about movable property, and a critical principle governs what happens if the pawn is destroyed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon: the method of taking possession (qabd) in a pawn contract. The ruling is that takhliya (enabling access) is a valid form of qabd.
This is compared to sale, where takhliya also suffices.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is an analogy (qiyas) with sale: 'as in sale' (كما في البيع). In sale, takhliya is accepted as delivery; by analogy, the same applies to pawn because both are exchange contracts requiring possession for bindingness.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The operative cause (illah) for accepting takhliya is that possession in consensual contracts is established by enabling the other party to take control, not necessarily by physical transfer.
The analogy holds unless a specific impediment exists, such as the nature of movable property (Abu Yusuf's objection).
4
Note the ikhtilaf (intra-Hanafi dispute)
Abu Yusuf holds that in movable property, actual physical transfer (naql) is required because qabd there creates liability similar to usurpation (ghasb), which demands physical taking.
This disagreement affects whether takhliya suffices for movables like a ring or a car.
5
Understand the daman principle
If the pawn perishes after valid qabd, the pawnee is liable for the lesser of the pawn's value and the debt. This ensures fairness: the pawnee does not profit from loss, and the pawner does not lose more than what he owes.
This rule applies regardless of the method of qabd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتخلية قبض فيه كما في البيع، وضمن بأقل من قيمته ومن الدين كالثمر على الشجر يجب أن يميز ويفصل عنه، فالمفرغ يتعلق بالمحل، فيجب فراغه عما حل فيه كالثمر، وهو ليس بمرهون سواء كان اتصاله به خلقة أو مجاورة، والمتميز يتعلق بالحال في المحل، فيجب انفصاله عن محل غير مرهون إذا كان اتصالُهُ به خلقة، حتى لو كان اتصاله بالمجاورة لا يضرّ كرهن المتاع الذي في بيت الراهن. (والتخلية قبض فيه كما في البيع، التخلية أن يضعه الراهن في موضع يتمكن المرتهن من أخذه، هذا في ظاهر الرواية، وعن أبي يوسف رحمه الله لا يثبت في المنقول إلا بالنقل ؛ لأنه قبض موجب للضمان بمنزلة الغصب.
And takhliya (enabling access) is a valid form of qabd (possession) in it (pledge) as in sale. And it is liable for the lesser of its value and the debt, like fruit on a tree must be distinguished and separated from it. So what is detached relates to the place, so it must be vacated from what has occurred in it like fruit, and it is not pledged whether its connection is by creation or adjacency. And what is distinguishable relates to the present state in the place, so it must be separated from a non-pledged place if its connection to it is by creation, even if its connection is by adjacency it does not harm, like pawning goods that are in the pawner's house. And takhliya is qabd in it as in sale. Takhliya is that the pawner places it in a location where the pawnee can take it. This is in the apparent riwayah. And from Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him): it is not established in movable property except by physical transfer, because it is a qabd that necessitates liability like usurpation.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of Al-Wiqaya presents the ruling on how possession (qabd) is established in a pawn, and the basis of liability (daman) when the pawn is destroyed. The passage begins by stating a foundational principle: in pawn contracts, takhliya (enabling the pawnee to take possession) suffices as qabd, just as in a sale contract. This is the Hanafi position in Zahir al-Riwayah. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that for movable property, actual physical transfer is required because the pawnee's hand gives rise to liability akin to usurpation (ghasb), and liability in movable property requires actual control. The passage also clarifies the status of items that are attached or separable: things that are attached by nature (like fruit on a tree) are not part of the pawn and must be separated, while things merely adjacent (like goods in the house) do not invalidate the pawn. The key ruling on liability is introduced: if the pawn perishes, the pawnee is liable for the lesser of the debt and the value of the pawn. This ensures fairness: the pawnee does not profit from a loss, nor does the pawner lose more than the debt.

Key Terms
التخلية (al-takhliya): The act of the pawner enabling the pawnee to take physical possession by placing the pawn in an accessible location.
قبض (qabd): Taking possession or control of the pawn by the pawnee, which establishes liability.
ضمن (daman): Liability or guarantee; the pawnee is responsible for the pawn's value in case of loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only by physical transfer of the pawn into the pawnee's hands
B) By the pawner enabling the pawnee to take it (takhliya)
C) By written agreement alone
D) By verbal declaration only

Key Takeaway
Takhliya (enabling access) is a valid form of qabd in pawn according to the dominant Hanafi view, and the pawnee's liability is limited to the lesser of the pawn's value and the debt.

Liability of the Pawnee When the Pawn Perishes: Hanafi Ruling and Inter-Madhhab Comparison
Elaborates the three outcomes when a pawn perishes and compares schools

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for pawnee liability when a pawn perishes, distinguish the three value/debt scenarios, and compare the Hanafi position with the Maliki and Shafi'i positions.

Core Idea
A pawn in the pawnee's possession is a hand of recovery; its destruction is treated as constructive recovery, limiting liability to the lesser of value and debt.

This passage completes the discussion on the liability of a pawn after destruction. It presents three clear scenarios based on the relation between the pawn's value and the debt, then explains the Hanafi rationale that the pawnee's possession is a 'yad istifa'' (hand of recovery). Finally, it contrasts the Hanafi view with those of Malik and al-Shafi'i, making this a concise inter-madhhab comparison.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Read the passage and note the three rules: when value equals debt, debt is discharged; when value exceeds debt, surplus is trust; when value is less, debt is reduced by value and remainder claimed.
Focus on the outcomes for each scenario.
2
Understand the rationale: yad istifa'
The author explains that the pawnee's hand is a 'hand of recovery' (yad istifa'). The pawn is held as a means to recover the debt, so its destruction is considered constructive recovery.
This rationale distinguishes the Hanafi view from others.
3
Articulate the Hanafi rule
State clearly: the pawnee is liable for the lesser of the debt and the pawn's value upon destruction. If value > debt, surplus is amanah; if value < debt, debt reduces by value.
This is the concise rule.
4
Compare with Maliki and Shafi'i
Malik holds the pawn is liable for full value (mabmun bil qimah); Shafi'i holds it is a pure trust (amanah) with no liability. Contrast their bases.
Note the extreme positions: full liability vs. no liability.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Work through a concrete example: pawn worth $100, debt $80. Hanafi: debt discharged, surplus $20 trust. Maliki: pawnee liable for $100? Shafi'i: no liability, pawner loses pawn and still owes debt.
The practical difference is significant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا سلم إليه وقبضه دخل في ضمانه. فلو هلك وهما سواء سقط دينه، وإن كانت قيمته أكثر، فالفضل أمانة، وفي أقل سقط من دينه بقدرها، ورجع المرتهن بالفضل. فالحاصل أن يد المرتهن على الرهن يد استيفاء؛ لأنه وثيقة لجانب الاستيفاء؛ لتكون موصلة إليه، فيكون استيفاء من وجه، ويتقرب بالهلاك، فإذا كان الدين أقل من القيمة فقد استوفى الدين، والفضل أمانة، وإن كانت القيمة أقل يكون مستوفياً بقدر المالية، وهي القيمة، فيرجع بالفضل هذا عندنا، وعند مالك مضمون بالقيمة، وعند الشافعي هو غير مضمون، بل هو أمانة.
So when he delivers it to him and he takes possession, it enters into his liability. So if it perishes while both (value and debt) are equal, his debt falls. If its value is greater, the surplus is a trust. If it is less, part of his debt falls by its amount, and the pawnee claims the remainder. The conclusion is that the pawnee's hand over the pawn is a hand of recovery (istifa'); because it is a document for the side of recovery, to be a means to it, so it is recovery in a sense, and becomes close through destruction. So if the debt is less than the value, he has recovered the debt, and the surplus is a trust. If the value is less, he has recovered to the extent of the financial worth, which is the value, so he claims the surplus. This is our position. And according to Malik, it is liable for the full value. And according to al-Shafi'i, it is not liable at all, rather it is a trust.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage completes the discussion on the liability of the pawn after destruction. It begins by reaffirming that once the pawnee takes possession, the pawn enters his liability (daman). Then it enumerates the three possible outcomes: (1) If the value of the pawn equals the debt, the debt is fully discharged; the pawnee loses nothing. (2) If the value exceeds the debt, the pawnee recovers his debt via the pawn, and the surplus must be returned to the pawner as an amanah (trust). (3) If the value is less than the debt, the pawnee recovers only the value of the pawn, and he may claim the remainder of the debt from the pawner. The passage then provides the underlying rationale: the pawnee's possession is a 'hand of recovery' (yad istifa'), meaning he holds the pawn as a means to recover his debt. Therefore, destruction is treated as constructive recovery: if the pawn perishes, it is as if he has recovered the value. Hence, he is liable only to the extent of the debt or the value, whichever is less. The Hanafi position is contrasted with two other schools: Imam Malik holds the pawnee liable for the full value of the pawn (regardless of the debt), while Imam Shafi'i considers the pawn a pure trust (amanah) in the pawnee's hands, so if it perishes without negligence, there is no liability at all. The passage thus presents a concise inter-madhhab dispute, clearly stating the Hanafi view and summarizing the opposing views without detailed rebuttal.

Key Terms
استيفاء (istifa'): The act of recovering a debt; the pawnee's possession is considered a tool for recovery.
أمانة (amanah): Trust; the surplus value beyond the debt is held in trust by the pawnee.
مضمون بالقيمة (madmun bi al-qimah): Liable for the full value of the pawn according to Imam Malik.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The debt is discharged, and the pawnee owes the pawner 20
B) The debt is discharged, and the surplus 20 is a trust with the pawnee
C) The debt remains, and the pawner loses the pawn
D) The pawnee must pay the pawner 100

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats the destruction of a pawn as constructive recovery, limiting the pawnee's liability to the lesser of the debt and the pawn's value.

Mortgagee's Rights: Demand, Imprisonment, and Detention
Explains mortgagee's rights with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three rights of a mortgagee (murtahin) and explain the conditions under which he may imprison the pledgor.

Core Idea
The mortgagee can demand debt, imprison for proven delay, and detain the pledge until full payment.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the legal entitlements of a mortgagee once a rahn contract is established. The mortgagee does not lose his right to seek the debt by entering into the pledge; rather, he gains the additional right to detain the pledged property. Understanding these rights is crucial for both the mortgagee's security and the pledgor's protection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rights
From the text: (1) demanding the debt, (2) imprisoning the pledgor, (3) detaining the pledge even after contract rescission.
These rights are permissions, not obligations.
2
Understand the imprisonment condition
Imprisonment is only when delay is proven; if the right is established by confession, no imprisonment. If by evidence, imprisonment occurs.
The judge determines proof of delay.
3
Examine the detention right
The mortgagee may detain the entire pledge until the debt is fully paid, even if partial payment is made. After full payment, he must return the pledge.
If the pledge perishes after full payment but before delivery, the pledgor recovers the payment.
4
Note the amanah status
The pledge remains amanah (trust) in the mortgagee's hands; if it perishes, he is not liable unless negligence.
This is a key point contested by Zufar (covered in next lesson).

Source Text

(And the mortgagee has the right to demand his debt from the pledgor, for the demand of the debt and imprisonment for it are not dropped by the pledge) i.e. detention of the pledge for the debt, and detention of his pledge after rescission of his contract until he receives his debt or discharges him, for it is not voided except by returning to the pledgor in a manner of rescission; because it remains guaranteed as long as possession and debt remain. His statement: 'and to imprison him for it' because his right remains after the pledge, and the pledge is for increased preservation so litigation is not prevented by it, and imprisonment is a penalty for injustice, so if his delay becomes apparent to the judge, he imprisons him, as established in the Book of Adab al-Qadi that he refrains from imprisonment if the right is established by his confession because it is not known that he is delaying, but if it is established by evidence he imprisons him, as his delay becomes apparent by his denial. His statement: 'until he receives his debt...' etc. i.e. if the pledgor wants to sell the pledge to pay the debt from its price, the mortgagee is not compelled to enable him to sell until he receives the debt, because the ruling of the pledge is permanent detention until the debt is paid, not payment from its price. If he pays part of it, he may detain all the pledge until he recovers the remainder as in detention of the sold item. When the pledgor pays the entire debt, the mortgagee surrenders the pledge to him because the impediment to surrender is removed due to the mortgagee's right being fulfilled. Said in Takmilat al-Bahr: if the pledge perishes after payment of the debt before its delivery to the pledgor, the pledgor recovers what he paid from the debt because it becomes apparent by the perishing that he had become (the debt) satisfied from the time of prior possession, so the second (payment) is satisfaction after satisfaction, so return is obligatory; and this is because by fulfilling the debt the pledge is not dissolved until he returns it to its owner, so it remains guaranteed in its state after payment of the debt as long as he does not deliver it to the pledgor or the mortgagee discharges him from the debt.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof and an intra-Hanafi dispute. It begins by stating the mortgagee's rights: he may demand his debt from the pledgor, have him imprisoned for non-payment, and detain the pledge even after rescinding the contract until the debt is paid or discharged. The commentary explains that these rights are not lost by entering into a rahn contract; the pledge remains in the mortgagee's possession as amanah (trust) as long as the debt exists. If the pledge perishes (by death, fire, drowning) it remains amanah, not guaranteed, according to Malik—but the text notes an issue in Hidayah where it says 'Zufar' instead of 'Malik.' It then presents Zufar's view: the pledge is guaranteed by value, so if the value exceeds the debt, the pledgor recovers the excess. Zufar cites a hadith from 'Ali (radiyallahu 'anhu) that they refund the surplus in the case of a pledge. The Hanafi majority responds: our school is based on reports from 'Umar and 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud; the mortgagee's hand is a hand of recovery, so he is only liable for what he actually recovers; the excess is detained only as a necessity to detain the principal, but there is no necessity to impose guarantee on it. The hadith about mutual return refers to the case of sale, not the case of perishing; the mortgagee is a trustee (amin) regarding the excess. The passage then explains the right to imprison: if the pledgor delays payment, the judge may imprison him, but if the right is established by confession, he is not imprisoned because his delay is not apparent; if by evidence, he is imprisoned. Finally, it discusses the right to detain until full payment: even if the pledgor pays part, the mortgagee may detain all the pledge; after full payment, he must surrender it. If the pledge is destroyed after payment but before delivery, the pledgor recovers the payment because the pledge was still in the mortgagee's possession and he is considered to have been satisfied from the time of prior possession, making the payment redundant.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A contract by which property is detained as security for a debt, without transferring ownership.
مرتهن (murtahin): The creditor/mortgagee who holds the pledge as security.
أمانة (amanah): A trust; the mortgagee is not liable for loss unless caused by his negligence or violation.
ضمان (daman): Liability/guarantee; the mortgagee would be liable for the full value if the pledge is guaranteed.
حبس (habs): Detention; the mortgagee's right to keep the pledge until the debt is paid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when can a mortgagee imprison the pledgor?
A) Immediately after the debt becomes due.
B) Only after the judge determines that the pledgor is delaying payment.
C) Only after the pledge is destroyed.
D) Never, because imprisonment is not allowed.

Key Takeaway
The mortgagee has the right to detain the pledge and to have the pledgor imprisoned for proven delay, but cannot sell or own the pledge.

The Amanah vs Daman Dispute: Is the Pledge a Trust or Guarantee?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on pledge's legal status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi majority position that the pledge is amanah, present Imam Zufar's opposing view that it is daman, and evaluate the evidence for each.

Core Idea
The pledge is amanah, not guaranteed; Zufar's view that it is daman is rejected by the Hanafi majority.

This passage delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the nature of the pledge (rahn) held by the mortgagee. While the majority of Hanafi scholars consider it a trust (amanah), Imam Zufar argues that it is guaranteed (daman) to the extent of its value. The resolution relies on reports from prominent Sahaba and a careful analysis of hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Majority: pledge is amanah – mortgagee not liable for loss beyond debt. Zufar: pledge is daman – mortgagee liable for full value, must pay excess to pledgor if pledge perishes.
2
Examine Zufar's evidence
Zufar cites the hadith of 'Ali (radiyallahu 'anhu): 'they mutually refund the excess in the pledge' and argues that the excess is also pledged and thus guaranteed.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The majority interprets the hadith as referring to the case of sale, not perishing. They cite other reports from 'Ali that the mortgagee is a trustee over the excess. Also, sahabi practice from 'Umar and Ibn Mas'ud supports amanah.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If the pledge perishes, under Zufar the pledgor recovers the excess value; under the majority, he does not. This affects risk allocation.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The majority view (amanah) is the relied-upon position in fatwa, based on stronger evidence and authority of the Sahaba.

And the mortgagee has the right to demand his debt from the pledgor and to imprison him for it and to detain his pledge after the rescission of his contract until he receives his debt or he discharges him. His statement: 'according to Malik...' etc. And in al-Hidayah: Zufar said: the pledge is guaranteed by value, so if the pledge perishes and its value on the day of pledging is one thousand five hundred and the debt is one thousand, the pledgor recovers from the mortgagee five hundred, citing the hadith of 'Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) who said: they mutually return the excess in the pledge, and because the excess over the debt is pledged because it is detained by it, so it becomes guaranteed by considering the measure of the debt. And our school is narrated from 'Umar and 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud; and because the hand of the mortgagee is a hand of recovery, so it does not necessitate guarantee except to the extent of what is recovered as in the reality of recovery, and the excess is pledged necessarily because of the impossibility of detaining the principal without it, and there is no necessity regarding guarantee, and what is meant by mutual return in what was narrated is the case of sale, for it was narrated from him that he said: the mortgagee is trustworthy regarding the excess. His statement: 'and to imprison him for it' because his right remains after the pledge, and the pledge is for increased preservation so litigation is not prevented by it, and imprisonment is a penalty for injustice, so if his delay becomes apparent to the judge, he imprisons him, as established in the Book of Adab al-Qadi that he refrains from imprisonment if the right is established by his confession because it is not known that he is delaying, but if it is established by evidence he imprisons him, as his delay becomes apparent by his denial. His statement: 'until he receives his debt...' etc. i.e. if the pledgor wants to sell the pledge to pay the debt from its price, the mortgagee is not compelled to enable him to sell until he receives the debt, because the ruling of the pledge is permanent detention until the debt is paid, not payment from its price. If he pays part of it, he may detain all the pledge until he recovers the remainder as in detention of the sold item. When the pledgor pays the entire debt, the mortgagee surrenders the pledge to him because the impediment to surrender is removed due to the mortgagee's right being fulfilled. Said in Takmilat al-Bahr: if the pledge perishes after payment of the debt before its delivery to the pledgor, the pledgor recovers what he paid from the debt because it becomes apparent by the perishing that he had become (the debt) satisfied from the time of prior possession, so the second (payment) is satisfaction after satisfaction, so return is obligatory; and this is because by fulfilling the debt the pledge is not dissolved until he returns it to its owner, so it remains guaranteed in its state after payment of the debt as long as he does not deliver it to the pledgor or the mortgagee discharges him from the debt.

Prohibition of Using the Pledge Without Permission
States the prohibition and consequences of unauthorized use of a pledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on using a pledged item without permission, explain its effect on the pledge contract, and apply the liability rules for use with and without permission.

Core Idea
Using a pledge without permission is haram but does not invalidate the rahn; it triggers full liability as a usurper.

When a creditor takes a pledge (rahn), they hold it as security, not as a source of benefit. This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya makes clear that the mortgagee may not use the pledged property in any way—whether by living in it, renting it, or wearing it—unless the pledgor explicitly permits it. Any unauthorized use constitutes transgression (ta'addi), but surprisingly, it does not void the rahn contract. The real consequence is a shift in liability: the mortgagee becomes a usurper and must pay the full value if the property perishes. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the Hanafi balance between keeping the contract intact and protecting the pledgor's rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited act and its ruling
Read the passage: the mortgagee is forbidden from benefiting (al-intifa') from the pledged item by using (istikhmad), dwelling (sukna), wearing (lubs), renting (ijarah), or lending (i'arah). Doing any of these makes him a transgressor (muta'addi) and the act is haram.
The prohibition is general; all forms of personal benefit are covered unless permission is given.
2
Determine the effect of transgression on the rahn contract
The text explicitly says: 'wa la yubtalu al-rahn bihi' – the pledge is not invalidated by this transgression. Even if the mortgagee uses the item, the security contract remains valid and the debt is still secured.
This is a key Hanafi principle: sin and transgression do not necessarily nullify a valid contract.
3
Distinguish between use with and without permission
When the pledgor grants permission, the mortgagee's possession changes from rahn (pledge) to ariyah (loan). If the item perishes during permitted use, nothing is deducted from the debt. Without permission, the mortgagee is considered a usurper (qabid bil-ghasb) and is liable for the full value (daman al-ghasb).
Permission transforms the legal nature of possession, altering liability.
4
Apply the consequence of unauthorized use and loss
If the mortgagee uses without permission and the item is destroyed (halaka), he must pay its full value. This liability is not offset against the debt; it is a separate obligation. If he stops using it, the item reverts to being a pure pledge.
The text from al-Mabsut states: 'wa law taraka al-isti'mal ada li-kawnihi rahnan' – if he stops using, it returns to being a pledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا الانتفاع به باستخدام ولا سكنى ولا لُبس ولا إجارة ولا إعارة، وهو متعد لو فعل، ولا يبطل الرهن به : أي بالتعدي. لا الانتفاع به... الخ أي لا يجوز الانتفاع بالرهن ؛ لأن الرهن يقتضي الحبس إلى أن يستوفي دينه دون الانتفاع ، فلا يجوز الانتفاع إلا بتسليط دين الراهن، وإن فعل كان متعدياً، ولا يبطل الرهن بالتعدي. قال في «المبسوط»: وليس للمرتهن أن ينتفع بالمرهون إلا بإذن الراهن، فإذا أذن له جاز أن يفعل ما أذن له فيه، ولو فعل من غير إذن صار ضامناً بحكم الرهن، وقابضاً للغصب، وإن ترك الاستعمال عاد لكونه رهناً. ولو استعمل المرتهن بإذن الراهن فإن هلك حالة الانتفاع لم يسقط من الدين شيء؛ لأنه بالإذن صار مقبوضاً بحكم العارية، وإن خالف وهلك في حال الاستعمال يضمن ضمان الغصب قوله : وهو متعد لو فعل ؛ فلو هلك به ضمنه ضمان الغصب بجميع قيمته.
No benefiting from it by using, nor by dwelling, nor by wearing, nor by renting, nor by lending, and he is a transgressor if he does so, and the pledge is not invalidated by it, i.e. by the transgression. 'No benefiting from it...' etc. i.e. it is not permissible to benefit from the pledge because the pledge necessitates detention until he recovers his debt, not benefiting. So it is not permissible to benefit except by authorization of the pledgor. If he does so, he is a transgressor, and the pledge is not invalidated by transgression. Said in al-Mabsut: The mortgagee may not benefit from the pledged property except with the pledgor's permission. If he permits him, it is permissible to do what he permitted. If he does so without permission, he becomes liable (damin) according to the ruling of the pledge, and is a possessor by usurpation (qabid bil-ghasb). If he leaves the use, it returns to being a pledge. If the mortgagee uses with the pledgor's permission and it perishes during use, nothing is deducted from the debt because by the permission it was possessed by the ruling of 'ariyah (loan). If he violates and it perishes during use, he guarantees it by the guarantee of usurpation (daman al-ghasb). His statement: 'and he is a transgressor if he does so'; so if it perishes thereby, he guarantees it by the guarantee of usurpation with its entire value.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement expanding on the matn: 'No benefiting from the pledge...' It clarifies that the mortgagee may not use the pledged item in any way—not by employing it as a servant, not by living in it, not by wearing it, not by renting it out, and not by lending it to someone else. If he does any of these, he is a transgressor (muta'addi). However, the pledge itself remains valid; it is not invalidated by his transgression. The commentary cites al-Mabsut: the mortgagee may only benefit from the pledge if the pledgor explicitly permits him. If permission is given, the use is permissible. If he uses without permission, he becomes liable (damin) under the law of the pledge, and is considered a usurper (ghasib). If he stops using it, it reverts to being a pledge in his possession. If he uses with permission and the property perishes, nothing is deducted from the debt because the property was effectively held under the rules of ariyah (loan). If he violates the permission (e.g., uses in a way not permitted) and it perishes, he must pay its full value as a usurper (daman al-ghasb).

Key Terms
الانتفاع (al-intifa'): Benefiting from the use or usufruct of the pledged property.
متعد (muta'addi): One who transgresses by using the pledge without permission; he becomes liable for damages.
ضمان الغصب (daman al-ghasb): The liability of a usurper to pay the full value of the property if it perishes while wrongfully in his possession.
عارية (ariyah): A loan of an item for use without rent; possession is with permission and the borrower is not liable for accidental loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The rahn becomes void (batil).
B) The rahn remains valid but the mortgagee is a transgressor and liable.
C) The rahn is suspended until the mortgagee repents.
D) The rahn becomes fasid and the mortgagee must return the item.

Key Takeaway
The mortgagee's unauthorized use is haram but does not invalidate the rahn; with permission the possession becomes ariyah, without it, the mortgagee is a usurper liable for full value.

Creditor's Use of the Pledged Property
Ruling on creditor's use of pledged property with and without permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the mortgagee's use of the pledged property and distinguish the legal consequences of permitted versus unpermitted use.

Core Idea
The mortgagee cannot use the pledge without permission; use without permission is a transgression but does not void the pledge.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies a key rule in Hanafi fiqh regarding the pledge (rahn). The mortgagee holds the property as security, not for personal benefit. The text explains the consequences of using the pledge with and without the owner's permission, and how liability shifts depending on the permissibility of use. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rulings of rahn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the mortgagee using the pledged property. The ruling is it is prohibited (haram) without permission.
2
Examine the exceptions
If the owner gives permission, the use becomes permissible and the pledge is treated as a loan (ariyah) during the use.
3
Understand the liability
Without permission, the mortgagee becomes a guarantor (damin) and liable for the full value if the property perishes; with permission, no liability for accidental loss.
4
Note the effect on the pledge contract
Use without permission does not invalidate the pledge; the contract remains valid.
5
Consider the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school allows use based on a hadith, but the Hanafi school prohibits it. The practical difference is liability and permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا الانتفاع به باستخدام ولا سكنى ولا لبس ولا إجارة ولا إعارة، وهو متعد لو فعل، ولا يبطل الرهن به : أي بالتعدي. قال في «المبسوط»: وليس للمرتهن أن ينتفع بالمرهون إلا بإذن الراهن، فإذا أذن له جاز أن يفعل ما أذن له فيه، ولو فعل من غير إذن صار ضامناً بحكم الرهن، وقابضاً للغصب، وإن ترك الاستعمال عاد لكونه رهناً. ولو استعمل المرتهن بإذن الراهن فإن هلك حالة الانتفاع لم يسقط من الدين شيء؛ لأنه بالإذن صار مقبوضاً بحكم العارية، وإن خالف وهلك في حال الاستعمال يضمن ضمان الغصب [٢] قوله : وهو متعد لو فعل ; فلو هلك به ضمنه ضمان الغصب بجميع قيمته
He may not benefit from it by use, nor by dwelling, nor by wearing, nor by renting, nor by lending; and he is a transgressor if he does so, and the pledge is not invalidated by it, i.e., by the transgression. He said in al-Mabsut: It is not for the mortgagee to benefit from the mortgaged property except with the permission of the mortgagor. If he permits him, he may do what he permitted. If he does so without permission, he becomes liable for the pledge, and takes possession as a usurper. If he refrains from use, it returns to being a pledge. And if the mortgagee uses with the permission of the mortgagor, and it perishes during the state of use, nothing is deducted from the debt; because by permission it becomes taken as a loan (ariyah). If he contravenes and it perishes while in use, he is liable with the liability of usurpation. [2] His statement: And he is a transgressor if he does so; so if it perishes thereby, he is liable for it with the liability of usurpation for its entire value.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the mortgagee's use of the pledged property. It states that the mortgagee is absolutely prohibited from using the pledged item – whether through personal use, renting, or lending – without the owner's permission. If he does use it without permission, he becomes a transgressor, but the pledge contract is not invalidated. The commentary from al-Mabsut clarifies that if the mortgagee uses it with permission, it becomes an 'ariyah (gratuitous loan) for that period; if it perishes during that permitted use, the mortgagee is not liable, but if he uses it without permission, he becomes a guarantor (damin) and must compensate for the entire value if it perishes.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): The pledge or collateral in a debt contract; an object held as security for a debt.
مرتهن (murtahin): The creditor/mortgagee who holds the pledge as security.
راهن (raahin): The debtor/mortgagor who pledges property as security.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if the mortgagee uses the pledged property without the mortgageor's permission?
A) The pledge becomes void
B) The mortgagee is a transgressor but the pledge remains valid
C) The mortgagee becomes the owner
D) The mortgagee must return the pledge immediately

Key Takeaway
The mortgagee's right over the pledge is limited to custody; any benefit without permission is a transgression that incurs liability but does not void the pledge contract.
