Invalid Mortgages: Undivided Shares and Attached Items
Explaining invalid mortgages of musha' and attached items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mortgaging an undivided share and attached items, explain the underlying reason based on perpetual detention, and distinguish the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Mortgage requires perpetual detention; an undivided share cannot be detained continuously, thus invalid.

Mortgage (rahn) is a contract designed to secure a debt by detaining an asset. The Hanafi school emphasizes that the mortgagee must have the right of continuous detention. This lesson examines two categories of invalid mortgages: undivided shares (musha') and items attached to property (like fruit on a tree). We will explore the legal reasoning, the difference between fasid and batil, and the contrasting view of Imam al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the invalid mortgages
The passage lists four invalid mortgages: an undivided share (musha'), fruit on a palm without the tree, crops on land without the land, and palm trees on land without the land. Recognize that the underlying reason is connected to the impossibility of separate possession.
These are summarised in the opening sentence.
2
Understand the Hanafi proof for musha'
The core argument is that the purpose of mortgage is perpetual detention (habis da'im). In an undivided share, co-owners could arrange alternating use (muhayah), which would defeat the continuity. Therefore, it is impossible to achieve the objective, making the mortgage invalid.
The text contrasts this with gift (hiba) and lease (ijara), whose objectives (ownership and use) can be achieved in a share.
3
Distinguish fasid from batil
The mortgage of an undivided share is fasid (irregular), not batil (void). Why? Because the item is property (mal) and the debt is guaranteed (mamun). The difference matters: if possession is taken, the mortgagee is liable for damages. This confirms that qabd is a condition for completion, not validity.
Some scholars incorrectly call it batil, but the correct Hanafi position is fasid.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i view
Imam al-Shafi'i considers the mortgage valid because he analogizes it to sale; since selling an undivided share is valid, mortgaging it should also be valid. The Hanafi response distinguishes the objectives: mortgage is about detention, not sale. Sale is only a resort in default.
This is a classic example of how different usul lead to different rulings.
5
Apply the rules to attached items
Items attached to land (fruit, crops, trees) cannot be mortgaged separately because they are not distinct; possession (qabd) of the item alone is impossible. However, if land is mortgaged, attached items are included by way of dependency to validate the contract, unlike sale which does not automatically include them.
The principle: if the mortgaged item is connected to something not mortgaged, separate possession fails, so the mortgage is invalid.

Source Text

لا يصح رهن مشاع وثمر على نخل دونه، وزرع أرض ونخل أرض دونها
[١] قوله : باب ما يصح رهنه ؛ لما ذكر مقدّمات مسائل الرهن ذكر في هذا الباب تفصيل ما يجوز رهنه والرهن به وما لا يجوز، إذ التفصيلُ إنّما يكون بعد الإجمال. كذا في «تكملة البحر). [٢]قوله : رهن مشاع ؛ أي لا يصح رهن المشاع ، فظاهره أنه لا فرق بين ما يحتملُ القسمة وما لا يحتمل، قال صاحب «العناية» (۲) : رهن المشاع قابل للقسمة وغيره فاسد يتعلق به الضمان إذا قبض. وقيل : باطل لا يتعلّق به الضمان، وليس بصحيح ؛ لأنَّ الباطل منه فيما إذا لم یكن الرهن مالاً ولم يكن المقابل به مضموناً، وما نحن فيه ليس كذلك، بناءً على أنّ القبض شرط تمام العقد لا شرط جوازه ، وقال الشافعي له : يجوز؛ لأنَّ موجبه عنده بيعه، والمشاع لا يمتنع بيعه. ولنا : إنّ موجبه ثبوت يد الاستيفاء، واستحقاق الحبس الدائم، ولا يتصور الحبس الدائم فيه ؛ لأنه يبطل بالمهاياة ،قبضه، كأنه رهنه يوماً ويوماً لا ؛ ولذا يستوي فيه ما يقبل القسمة وما لا يقبلها، بخلاف الهبة حيث تجوزُ فيما لا يحتمل القسمة ؛ لأنَّ موجبها الملك ، ولا يمتنع بالشيوع. ولا يجوز من شريكه أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ ثبوت اليد في المشاع لا يتصور ؛ ولأنه لو جاز لأمسكه يوماً بحكم الرهن ويوماً بحكم الملك ، فيصير كأنه رهنه يوماً ويوماً لا ، بخلاف الإجارة حيث تجوز في المشاع من الشريك ؛ لأنَّ حكمها التمكن من الانتفاع لا الحبس، والشريك متمكن من ذلك. والشيوع الطارئ يمنعُ بقاء الرهن في رواية الأصل، وعن أبي يوسف له لا يمنع ؛ لأنَّ حكم البقاء أسهل من الابتداء، فأشبه الهبة. كتاب الرهن / تعريفه وشروطه وما يصح به والتصرف فيه
وكذا عكسها يصح (۲) ۳۷۷ ا رهن نخل بدون ثمر، وأرض لعدم كونه متميزاً، وكذا عكسها) : أي لا . بدون زرع أو نخل ؛ لعدم كونه مفرغاً ، فلا يتم القبض، وعن أبي حنيفة وجه رواية «الأصل» أنَّ الامتناع لعدم المحلية، وفي مثله يستوي الابتداء والبقاء، بخلاف الهبة ؛ لأنَّ المشاع لا يمنعُ حكمها، وهو الملك والمنع في الابتداء لنفي الغرامة، ولا حاجة إلى اعتباره في حالة البقاء ؛ ولهذا يـصـح الرجوع في بعض الموهوب ، ولا يصح الفسخ في بعض المرهون. تكملة البحر».
[١] قوله : لعدم كونه متميّزاً ؛ والقبض شرط في الرهن على ما مرَّ غير مرة، ولا يمكن قبض المتصل وحده، فصار في معنى المشاع، وفي رواية عن أبي حنيفة له : إنّ رهن الأرض دون الشجر جائز ؛ لأنَّ الشجر اسم النبات، فيكون استثناء الأشجار بمواضعها ، بخلاف ما إذا رهن الدار دون البناء ؛ لأنَّ البناء اسم للمبني، فتكون الأرض جميعاً رهناً، وهي مشغولة بملك الراهن. ولو رهن النخل بمواضعها جاز ؛ لأنه رهنَ الأرض بما فيها من النخل، وذلك جائز، ومجاورة ما ليس برهن لا يمنع الصحة، ويدخل في رهن الأرض النخل والثمر على النخل والزرع والرطبة والبناء والغرس ؛ لأنه تابع لاتصاله، فيدخل تبعاً تصحيحاً للعقد. بخلاف البيع، حيث لا تدخلُ هذه في بيع الأرض سوى النخل ؛ لأن بيع الأرض بدون هذه الأشياء جائز، فلا حاجة إلى إدخالها في البيع من غير ذكر. كذا في تكملة البحر» (٢). [٢] قوله : لا يصح؛ لأنَّ الأتصال بالطرفين، والأصل فيه : إنّ المرهون إذا كان متصلاً بما ليس بمرهون لم يجز ؛ لأنه لا يمكن قبض المرهون وحده، والقبض شرط في الرهن. (۳) قوله : لعدم كونه مفرغاً ؛ إشارة إلى تعليل جميع هذه المذكورات بالأصل (۱) تكملة البحر» (۸: ١٧٥ - ١٧٦). (۲) تكملة البحر» (۸: ١٧٦).
Chapter on what is valid to mortgage and the mortgage with it and what is not valid. It is not valid to mortgage an undivided share, and fruit on a palm tree without it, and crops on land and palm trees on land without it. [1] His statement: Chapter on what is valid to mortgage; since he mentioned the preliminaries of mortgage issues, in this chapter he mentions the details of what is permissible to mortgage and to mortgage with it and what is not permissible, because detail only comes after summary. As in 'Takmilat al-Bahr'. [2] His statement: mortgage of an undivided share; meaning it is not valid to mortgage an undivided share. Its apparent meaning is that there is no difference between what can be divided and what cannot. The author of al-'Inayah said: The mortgage of an undivided share, whether divisible or not, is fasid (irregular); liability for damages attaches if possession is taken. Some said it is batil (void) with no liability, but that is not correct because the void case is when the mortgaged item is not property and the countervalue is not guaranteed, which is not the case here, based on the view that possession is a condition for completion of the contract, not a condition for its validity. Al-Shafi'i said: It is valid, because in his view its consequence is sale, and an undivided share is not prevented from sale. Our evidence: Its consequence is the establishment of the hand of utilization and entitlement to perpetual detention, and perpetual detention is inconceivable in it because it is nullified by alternating use; it is as if he mortgaged it for a day and not for a day. Hence it is the same for divisible and indivisible shares, contrary to gift which is valid for indivisible shares because its consequence is ownership, and ownership is not prevented by co-ownership. It is also not valid from a partner because fixing the hand in an undivided share is inconceivable; and if it were valid, he would hold it one day by virtue of mortgage and one day by virtue of ownership, making it as if he mortgaged it for a day and not for a day. Contrary to lease, which is valid for an undivided share from a partner because its ruling is enabling utilization, not detention, and the partner is able to do that. Subsequent co-ownership prevents the continuation of the mortgage in the narration of al-Asl, and from Abu Yusuf it does not prevent because the ruling of continuation is easier than initiation, so it resembles a gift. (1) Takmilat al-Bahr (8:275). (2) al-'Inayah (10:157-158). [PAGE_377] Book of Mortgage: Its definition, conditions, what is valid to mortgage and in it, and disposal. And likewise its opposite is valid. (2) 377. Mortgage of palm trees without fruit, and land without crops or palm trees due to lack of distinctness, and likewise its opposite is not valid; due to lack of emptying, so possession is not completed. From Abu Hanifa there is a narration in al-Asl that the prevention is due to lack of suitability, and in such cases initiation and continuation are equal, contrary to gift because undivided share does not prevent its ruling, which is ownership, and prevention in initiation is to negate liability, and there is no need to consider it in the state of continuation; hence revoking part of a gifted item is valid, but annulment of part of a mortgaged item is not. Takmilat al-Bahr. [1] His statement: Due to lack of distinctness; and possession is a condition in mortgage as mentioned repeatedly, and it is impossible to possess the connected item alone, so it becomes in the meaning of an undivided share. In a narration from Abu Hanifa: Mortgage of land without trees is valid because trees are the name of plants, so it is an exception of trees in their places, contrary to mortgaging a house without the building because the building is the name of the built structure, so the entire land is mortgaged and it is occupied by the owner's property. If one mortgages the palm trees in their places, it is valid because he mortgaged the land with what is in it of palm trees, and that is permissible, and adjacency of what is not mortgaged does not prevent validity. In the mortgage of land, palm trees, fruit on palm trees, crops, pastures (ratbah), buildings, and plantings are included because they are dependent due to their connection, so they are included as follows to validate the contract. Contrary to sale, where these are not included in the sale of land except for palm trees; because the sale of land without these items is permissible, so there is no need to include them in the sale without mention. As in Takmilat al-Bahr (2). [2] His statement: It is not valid; because the connection on both sides, and the principle in it: if the mortgaged item is connected to what is not mortgaged, it is not valid because the mortgaged item alone cannot be possessed, and possession is a condition in mortgage. (3) His statement: Due to lack of emptying; indicating the reason for all these mentioned items according to the principle (1) Takmilat al-Bahr (8:175-176). (2) Takmilat al-Bahr (8:176).
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is an explication of the chapter on what is valid to mortgage and what is not, from the book 'Umda al-Ri'aya. It begins by stating the general ruling that four types of mortgage are invalid: (1) an undivided share (musha'), (2) fruit on a palm tree without the tree itself, (3) crops on land without the land, and (4) palm trees on land without the land itself. The commentator then expands on each. The primary issue is the mortgage of an undivided share. The author of al-'Inayah clarifies that the mortgage is fasid (irregular) and if possession is taken, the mortgagee is liable for damages. The correct position is that it is fasid, not batil, because the mortgaged item is property and the debt is guaranteed, so the condition for batil is absent. The key proof for the Hanafi position is that the purpose of mortgage is to give the mortgagee a right of perpetual detention (habis da'im), which is impossible in an undivided share because the parties could alternate use (muhayah), thereby defeating the continuity. This is contrasted with gift (hiba), which is valid for indivisible shares because its objective is ownership, not detention. Lease (ijara) is also valid for an undivided share from a partner because its objective is use, not detention. The text then discusses the case of subsequent co-ownership (shuyu' tari'i): in the narration of al-Asl, it breaks the mortgage, but Abu Yusuf holds it does not, analogizing to gift. The commentary also covers the rulings on mortgaging land without trees or buildings, and the inclusion of attached items in a land mortgage. A key principle is that possession (qabd) is a condition for completion of the mortgage, and if the item is physically connected to something not mortgaged, separate possession is impossible, rendering the mortgage invalid. However, if the land is mortgaged, attached items like trees and buildings are included by way of dependency to validate the contract, unlike sale where such items are not automatically included.

Key Terms
رهن مشاع (rahn musha'): A mortgage where the item is an undivided share (shuyu') of property, not a specific portion.
قبض (qabd): Physical possession taken by the mortgagee as a condition for the completion of the mortgage contract.
مهاياة (muhayah): Alternating use or allocation of time between co-owners, which defeats the perpetual detention required in mortgage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is mortgaging an undivided share (musha') invalid?
A) Because the share is not owned by the mortgagor.
B) Because perpetual detention cannot be maintained due to the possibility of alternating use.
C) Because the share is not a valid property.
D) Because possession cannot be taken at all.

Key Takeaway
The mortgage of an undivided share and attached items is invalid in Hanafi fiqh because the requirement of perpetual detention (habis da'im) cannot be satisfied, and separate possession is impossible for attached items.

Mortgaging Land Without Trees: Invalidity Due to Failure of Qabd
Ruling on validity of mortgage of land without trees or trees without land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mortgaging land without its trees and explain why the condition of qabd cannot be fulfilled.

Core Idea
Mortgaging attached property without separation invalidates the contract – qabd requires distinct possession.

Can you mortgage a piece of land but keep the trees for yourself? The Hanafi school says no – the attached trees make it impossible to take exclusive possession of the land alone. This lesson explores the reasoning behind that ruling and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The passage explicitly states that mortgaging land without its trees (or vice versa) is invalid (la yasihh). Recognize that the hukm is batil (void).
This is the central ruling of this passage.
2
Explain Why Qabd Fails
The reason given is that the mortgaged item is not distinct (mutamayyiz). Since the land and trees are attached physically, you cannot take the land into your exclusive possession without also taking the trees, which are not part of the mortgage. Qabd (possession) is a condition for the validity of a mortgage, and it cannot be fulfilled partially.
Compare with musha' (common property) – similar problem of indivisibility.
3
Understand the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa reportedly held that the prevention is due to lack of suitability (adam al-mahalliyya) – the attached part is not a valid subject of mortgage. The commentarial tradition notes that regardless of the reason, the ruling is the same: invalid.
This difference in rationale does not affect the outcome.
4
Distinguish from Gift (Hiba)
The passage contrasts mortgage with gift. In hiba, common property (musha') does not prevent the transfer of ownership, and revocation of part is valid. In mortgage, however, the need for qabd at the beginning makes partial mortgage impossible; once qabd is achieved, partial rescission is also invalid.
This distinction explains why the rule for mortgage is stricter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا عكسها لا يصح. قوله: لعدم كونه متميزاً؛ والقبض شرط في الرهن على ما مر غير مرة، ولا يمكن قبض المتصل وحده، فصار في معنى المشاع. وعن أبي حنيفة وجه رواية «الأصل» أنَّ الامتناع لعدم المحلية، وفي مثله يستوي الابتداء والبقاء، بخلاف الهبة؛ لأنَّ المشاع لا يمنع حكمها، وهو الملك والمنع في الابتداء لنفي الغرامة، ولا حاجة إلى اعتباره في حالة البقاء؛ ولهذا يصح الرجوع في بعض الموهوب، ولا يصح الفسخ في بعض المرهون. (قوله: لا يصح؛ لأنَّ الأتصال بالطرفين، والأصل فيه: إنَّ المرهون إذا كان متصلاً بما ليس بمرهون لم يجز؛ لأنه لا يمكن قبض المرهون وحده، والقبض شرط في الرهن.)
And likewise its opposite is not valid. His saying: Because it is not distinguishable; and possession is a condition in mortgage as has been mentioned many times, and it is not possible to take possession of the attached [item] alone, so it becomes like common property. And from Abu Hanifa there is a narration in al-Asl that the prevention is due to lack of suitability [for being a subject of mortgage], and in such a case the beginning and the continuation are equal, unlike gift; because common property does not prevent its ruling, which is ownership, and the prevention at the beginning is to negate liability for loss, and there is no need to consider it in the state of continuation; and for this reason revocation of part of a gifted item is valid, but rescission of part of a mortgaged item is not valid. His saying: It is not valid because the connection is from both sides, and the principle is that if the mortgaged item is attached to something not mortgaged, it is not permissible because it is not possible to take possession of the mortgaged item alone, and possession is a condition in mortgage.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on mortgaging land without trees or trees without land. The author states that such a mortgage is invalid. The reason given is that the item to be mortgaged is not distinct from the non-mortgaged item, making it impossible to take exclusive possession (qabd) of the mortgaged property, and possession is a condition for the validity of a mortgage. The principle is that if the mortgaged item is attached to something that is not mortgaged, the mortgage is invalid because qabd of the mortgaged item alone is impossible. The passage also notes a narration from Abu Hanifa that the prevention is due to lack of eligibility (mahalliyya) of the attached item for mortgage, and in such cases the ruling applies both at the start and during continuation, unlike a gift where common property does not prevent the ruling and revocation of part is allowed. The commentary clarifies that connection from both sides prevents valid qabd, and this is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Taking possession of the mortgaged item exclusively, which is a condition for validity of mortgage.
متميز (mutamayyiz): Distinct or separable from other property; here, the mortgaged item must be distinguishable from non-mortgaged items for valid qabd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is mortgaging land without its trees considered invalid in the Hanafi school?
A) Because trees are worth more than land.
B) Because the mortgaged item cannot be taken into exclusive possession without the trees.
C) Because trees are considered separate property.
D) Because the mortgage contract must include all property on the land.

Key Takeaway
Mortgaging land without its trees is invalid because exclusive possession (qabd) is impossible due to physical attachment – a condition for valid mortgage cannot be met.

Inclusion of Trees and Crops in Mortgage of Land
Ruling on inclusion of attached items in mortgage, distinguishing from sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on automatic inclusion of attached items in a mortgage of land and distinguish it from the rule in sale.

Core Idea
In mortgage, attached items enter automatically to perfect possession; in sale, they do not—except palm trees.

When you mortgage a piece of land, what exactly is being mortgaged? The Hanafi school has a clear answer: not just the soil, but everything attached to it—trees, crops, buildings. This lesson unpacks the rationale behind this automatic inclusion and contrasts it with the sale of land, where the rule is different.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Recognize that mortgaging land with its attached items (like palm trees) is valid. The ruling is that the mortgage includes all attached items automatically.
The act is rahn (mortgage) of land with attached items.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is the reasoning (ta'lil) that attached items are accessories (taba') and enter automatically to validate the contract (tashih al-aqd).
The Hanafi position is based on the need for complete possession (qabd) in mortgage.
3
Note the exception in sale
In sale, only palm trees enter automatically; other items like crops and buildings are not included unless specified.
The reason is that sale without these items is valid, so there is no need to force inclusion.
4
Analyze the rationale for the difference
Mortgage requires possession (qabd) of the mortgaged property as a condition for validity; including all attached items makes possession feasible. Sale does not require possession, so automatic inclusion is unnecessary.
The underlying principle: al-taba'iyyah (accessoriness) applies where the main contract's purpose requires it.
5
Derive the practical consequence (thamarah)
If someone mortgages land without mentioning the house, the house is automatically included. If they sell land without mentioning the house, the house is not included unless specified.
This difference affects drafting of contracts and the rights of parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو رهن النخل بمواضعها جاز؛ لأنه رهنَ الأرض بما فيها من النخل، وذلك جائز، ومجاورة ما ليس برهن لا يمنع الصحة، ويدخل في رهن الأرض النخل والثمر على النخل والزرع والرطبة والبناء والغرس؛ لأنه تابع لاتصاله، فيدخل تبعاً تصحيحاً للعقد. بخلاف البيع، حيث لا تدخلُ هذه في بيع الأرض سوى النخل؛ لأن بيع الأرض بدون هذه الأشياء جائز، فلا حاجة إلى إدخالها في البيع من غير ذكر.
And if one mortgages palm trees with their places, it is permissible because he mortgaged the land with what is in it of palm trees, and that is permissible, and contiguity of what is not mortgaged does not prevent validity. And included in the mortgage of land are palm trees, fruit on palm trees, crops, fresh greens, buildings, and plantings; because they are accessories due to their attachment, so they enter as accessories to validate the contract. Unlike sale, where these items do not enter into the sale of land except for palm trees; because the sale of land without these items is permissible, so there is no need to include them in the sale without mention.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage explains that when land is mortgaged, all attached items such as palm trees, fruit, crops, buildings, and plantings are automatically included as accessories (taba'). This is done to validate the mortgage contract, because without them the mortgage might be invalid due to inability to take possession. However, in the case of sale, only palm trees are included automatically, because a sale of land without those items is valid, so there is no need to include them unless specified. The distinction is based on the purpose: mortgage requires completeness for possession, while sale does not.

Key Terms
تبعاً (taba'an): As accessories; items that are attached to the main mortgaged property are included automatically.
تابع (tabi'): Accessory or follower; an item that is subordinate and attached to the principal property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are attached items like buildings and crops included in a mortgage of land but not in a sale?
A) Because mortgage requires possession of the entire property, while sale does not.
B) Because buildings are considered more valuable than land.
C) Because the buyer must explicitly request them.
D) Because the mortgage contract is more formal than sale.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when land is mortgaged, all attached items are automatically included as accessories to enable complete possession; this differs from sale, where only palm trees are included automatically.

Invalidity of Mortgaging Free Persons and Slaves with Partial Freedom
Ruling with proof on invalidity of mortgaging certain individuals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on mortgaging free persons, mudabbar, mukatab, and umm walad, and explain why their lack of saleability invalidates the mortgage.

Core Idea
Mortgage requires saleability; free persons and slaves with partial freedom cannot be sold, so mortgage is invalid.

This passage explains that it is invalid to mortgage a free person, a mudabbar, a mukatab, or an umm walad. The reason is that mortgage gives the creditor the right to sell the collateral if the debt is unpaid, but these individuals cannot be sold — the free person is not property, and the others have a right to freedom that blocks sale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Recognize that the passage lists four categories: free person, mudabbar, mukatab, and umm walad. The ruling for each is batil (invalid).
2
Understand the nature of mortgage
Mortgage (rahn) establishes a right for the creditor to take possession and eventually sell the collateral to recover the debt. This is the core purpose.
The Arabic phrase 'ثبوت يد الاستيفاء' refers to the right to appropriate.
3
Identify the impediment
For the free person, the impediment is lack of property value (la malīyah). For the others, it is the right to freedom (haqq al-ḥurriyah) that prevents sale.
The mudabbar, mukatab, and umm walad all have partial freedom that makes them unsellable.
4
Apply the principle
Since sale is not possible for these individuals, the mortgage is invalid. The lack of saleability is the decisive factor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورهن الحر والمدير والمكاتب وأم الولد. قوله: ورهن الحر...الخ؛ أي لا يصح رهن هؤلاء؛ لأنَّ حكمه ثبوت يد الاستيفاء، ولا يتصور استيفاء الدين من هذه الأعيان؛ لأنه لا يجوز بيعها، أما الحر فلعدم المالية، وأما الباقون فلقيام المانع فيهم، وهو حق الحرية؛ ولهذا لو طرأت عليه هذه التصرفات أبطلته.
And the mortgage of a free person, a mudabbar, a mukatab, and an umm walad. His saying: 'And the mortgage of a free person...etc.' meaning it is not valid to mortgage these individuals; because its ruling is the establishment of the right of appropriation, and the recovery of the debt from these entities cannot be conceived because their sale is not permissible. As for the free person, due to lack of property value; as for the others, due to an impediment existing in them, which is the right of freedom; and for this reason, if these dispositions were to occur, it would invalidate [the mortgage].
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage states that mortgaging a free person, a mudabbar (a slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death), a mukatab (a slave who has entered a contract for manumission), and an umm walad (a slave woman who has borne a child to her master) is invalid. The reason is that the purpose of mortgage is to secure a debt by giving the creditor the right to sell the property if the debt is not paid. However, these individuals cannot be sold: the free person is not property, and the others have a right to freedom that prevents their sale. Therefore, mortgage is not valid.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death; cannot be sold or mortgaged.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract to purchase his freedom; also cannot be sold or mortgaged.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master; she becomes free upon the master's death and cannot be sold during his life.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it invalid to mortgage a mudabbar slave?
A) Because the mudabbar is free immediately.
B) Because the mudabbar cannot be sold due to the right of freedom.
C) Because the mudabbar is not owned by the master.
D) Because the mudabbar is considered property only after the master's death.

Key Takeaway
Mortgage of any individual who cannot be sold — due to lack of property value or a right to freedom — is invalid.

Mortgaging Upon Trusts – Invalid
Ruling on mortgaging trust properties with reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between properties that can and cannot be mortgaged in the Hanafi school based on the requirement of guarantee (daman).

Core Idea
Only guaranteed (madhmun) properties can be validly mortgaged; trusts lack guarantee and are invalid.

This lesson examines a foundational principle in Hanafi mortgage law: the mortgaged property must be guaranteed (madhmun). We explore why trusts such as deposits, borrowed items, and partnership capital cannot serve as collateral, and contrast them with properties that are inherently guaranteed, like usurped goods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subject
The passage states that mortgage upon trusts (amanat) is invalid. Identify the specific trusts mentioned: deposit (wadi'ah), borrowed item (ariyah), mudaraba capital, and partnership property.
The ruling is one of impermissibility; the contract is batil.
2
Understand the underlying rationale (illah)
The reason is that possession (qabd) in mortgage must be with guarantee (madhmun). Trusts are not guaranteed; if lost without negligence, the trustee is not liable. Without the element of guarantee, the creditor cannot secure repayment from the property.
The illah is the absence of daman (guarantee).
3
Distinguish guaranteed vs. non-guaranteed properties
Contrast trusts with properties guaranteed in themselves, such as usurped goods, mahr, khul' compensation, etc. For these, the holder is liable for the value whether the property exists or not, so mortgage is valid.
The key is whether the property is madhmun (guaranteed) by its nature.
4
Consider the cross-madhhab perspective
Other schools may allow mortgage upon trusts if the creditor takes possession with permission. The Hanafi school, however, strictly requires guarantee, making such mortgages invalid.
This ikhtilaf has practical implications for fatwa.
5
Apply the rule to new cases
Using the criterion of guarantee, assess whether a given property can be mortgaged. For example, a security deposit held by a landlord is a trust and cannot be mortgaged, whereas a car loan collateral (where lender holds title) is guaranteed.
Always check if the property is madhmun.

Source Text

ولا بالأمانات: كالوديعة، والمستعار ومال المضاربة، والشركة. قوله: كالوديعة ... الخ؛ لأنَّ القبض في «باب الرهن» قبض مضمون، فلا بد من ضمان ثابت ليقع القبض مضموناً، ويتحقق استيفاء الدين منه، وكذلك لا يصح بالأعيان المضمونة بغيرها، كالمبيع في يد البائع؛ لأن الضمان ليس بواجب، فإنه إذا هلك العين لم يضمن البائع شيئاً، لكنه يسقط الثمن، وهو حق البائع، فلا يصح الرهن. فأما الأعيان المضمونة بعينها، وهي أن يكون مضموناً بالمثل أو بالقيمة عند هلاكه مثل: المغصوبة وبدل الخلع والمهر وبدل الصلح يصح الرهن بها؛ لأنَّ الضمان متقرر، فإنه وإن كان قائماً وجب تسليمه، وإن كان هالكا تجب قيمته، فكان رهناً بما هو مضمون فيصح.
And not upon trusts: like deposit, borrowed item, mudaraba capital, and partnership. His saying: like deposit...etc. because possession (qabd) in the chapter of mortgage is possession with guarantee (madhmun), so there must be a fixed guarantee for the possession to be with guarantee, and recovery of debt from it to be realized. Similarly, it is not valid upon properties that are guaranteed by others, like the sold item in the seller's hand; because the guarantee is not obligatory, for if the property perishes, the seller does not guarantee anything, but the price is canceled, which is the seller's right, so the mortgage is not valid. As for properties guaranteed in themselves, i.e., they are guaranteed by like or value upon destruction, such as usurped property, khul' compensation, dower, and compromise compensation, mortgage with them is valid; because the guarantee is established, for if it exists, it must be delivered, and if it perishes, its value is due, so it is a mortgage upon something that is guaranteed, so it is valid.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage explains that it is invalid to mortgage a property that is held as a trust (amanah), such as a deposit (wadi'ah), a borrowed item (ariyah), mudaraba capital, or partnership property. The reason is that in mortgage, the creditor must have a right to take possession with guarantee (daman) so that if the property is lost, the debtor is liable. Trusts are not guaranteed; if they perish without negligence, the trustee is not liable. Therefore, they cannot serve as security. In contrast, properties that are guaranteed by their nature (e.g., usurped goods, mahr, khul' compensation) can be mortgaged because the guarantor is liable for them whether they exist or not.

Key Terms
أمانات (amanat): Properties held in trust where the holder is not liable for loss unless negligent.
مضمون (madhmun): Guaranteed; property for which the holder is liable if lost.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following can be validly mortgaged in the Hanafi school?
A) A borrowed book (ariyah).
B) A deposit (wadi'ah).
C) A usurped item (maghsub).
D) Partnership capital (shirkah).

Key Takeaway
For a mortgage to be valid in the Hanafi school, the mortgaged property must be guaranteed (madhmun); trusts lack this element and cannot serve as collateral.

Invalid Collateral: Free Persons and Slaves with Partial Freedom
Ruling on invalidity of pledging free persons and certain categories of slaves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pledging free persons and certain categories of slaves and explain the underlying legal reasoning (talil).

Core Idea
A pledge requires saleable collateral; free persons and slaves with freedom rights cannot be sold, so their pledge is void.

In Hanafi law, a valid pledge (rahn) must give the creditor the right to sell the collateral if the debt is unpaid. This lesson examines four categories of individuals who cannot be pledged because they cannot be sold: free persons, mudabbar, mukatab, and umm walad. The passage explains the precise reason for each, building our understanding of the essential condition of saleability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage states that pledging a free person, mudabbar, mukatab, and umm walad is invalid. Note the ruling is 'batil' (void from the outset).
The ruling applies to all four equally, but for different reasons.
2
Understand the purpose of a pledge
The text mentions 'yad al-istifa'' – the creditor's right to take possession and sell the collateral to recover the debt. This is the essential legal objective (maqsad) of a pledge.
If sale is impossible, the pledge fails its purpose.
3
Analyze why each category cannot be sold
For a free person, the reason is lack of financial value (adam al-maliyya) – a free human is not property. For the others, the impediment (mani') is a right of freedom (haqq al-hurriyya) that prevents sale: the mudabbar's freedom is contingent on the master's death, the mukatab has a contract of manumission, and the umm walad cannot be sold because she is the mother of the master's child.
The impediment is a legal right, not a physical barrier.
4
Conclude the invalidity
Since the collateral cannot be sold, the condition necessary for a valid pledge is absent. Thus the contract is void.
This is a rational proof (talil) based on the maqsad of the contract.
5
Apply the principle to later-acquired status
The text notes that if a slave attains one of these statuses after being pledged, the pledge becomes invalid – the same illa (impediment) applies retroactively.
This shows the ruling is based on the status of the collateral at the time of enforcement, not just at contract formation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ورهن الحر والمدبر والمكاتب وأم الولد)[١] قوله: ورهن الحر...الخ؛ أي لا يصح رهن هؤلاء؛ لأنَّ حكمه ثبوت يد الاستيفاء، ولا يتصور استيفاء الدين من هذه الأعيان؛ لأنه لا يجوز بيعها، أما الحر فلعدم المالية، وأما الباقون فلقيام المانع فيهم، وهو حق الحرية؛ ولهذا لو طرأت عليه هذه التصرفات أبطلته).
And the pledge of a free person, the mudabbar, the mukatab, and the umm walad (is invalid). [1] His statement: 'and the pledge of a free person... etc.' means: the pledge of these individuals is not valid because the ruling of a pledge is the establishment of a hand of recovery (of debt), and recovery of the debt from these entities cannot be conceived because their sale is not permitted. As for the free person, it is due to lack of financial value; as for the others, it is due to the existence of an impediment in them, which is the right of freedom. Therefore, if these dispositions occur (to a slave), they invalidate the pledge.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: it is invalid to pledge a free person, a mudabbar (a slave whose freedom is contingent on the master's death), a mukatab (a slave who has made a contract of manumission), or an umm walad (a slave woman who has borne her master's child). The reason given is that the purpose of a pledge is to have a right of recovery (يد الاستيفاء) over the collateral, meaning the creditor can sell it to recover the debt. However, these individuals cannot be sold: the free person has no market value (مالية) as a commodity, and the others have an impediment (مانع) in the form of a right to freedom (حق الحرية) that prevents their sale. The Hanafi school holds that if a slave acquires such statuses after being pledged, the pledge becomes invalid. This is a rational proof (talil) based on the legal objective of a pledge.

Key Terms
المدبر (almudabbar): A slave whose manumission is deferred until after the master's death, as per the master's declaration.
المكاتب (almukatab): A slave who has entered a contract of kitabah (manumission by payment) with his master.
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A slave woman who has given birth to a child recognized as the master's.
يد الاستيفاء (yad al-istifa'): The right of the creditor to take possession of the collateral and sell it to recover the debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Lack of financial value
B) Existence of a right of freedom
C) Inability to possess the free person
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
A pledge is only valid if the collateral can be sold to recover the debt; any legal impediment to sale renders the pledge void.

Invalid Basis for Pledge: Trust Items
Ruling that trust items cannot secure a pledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pledging for trust items and explain the underlying rationale regarding guaranteed possession.

Core Idea
A pledge is only valid for a guaranteed debt; trust items lack guarantee, so they cannot be secured.

In the chapter on pledge (rahn), the Hanafi school establishes a key condition: the possession taken by the creditor must be a 'guaranteed possession' (qabd madmun). This means that the debt being secured must be a financial obligation that is guaranteed, i.e., the debtor is liable to pay it regardless of loss. The present passage clarifies that debts arising from trust items (amanat) such as deposits, loans, mudarabah, and partnership capital are not guaranteed—if the item perishes, the trustee is not liable. Consequently, one cannot validly pledge collateral for such a debt. The passage further distinguishes items that are guaranteed by themselves (like usurped goods) which can serve as a valid basis for pledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The passage begins by stating that a pledge cannot be taken for trust items (amanat). Recognize the ruling: invalid (batil).
The author lists examples: deposit (wadi'ah), borrowed item (ariyah), mudarabah capital, partnership capital.
2
Understand the Reason: The Need for Guaranteed Possession
The rationale is that possession in a pledge must be 'guaranteed' (qabd madmun). This means the creditor must be able to recover the debt from the collateral. If the debt itself is not guaranteed, the collateral cannot serve its purpose.
Trust items are not guaranteed because the holder is not liable if it perishes.
3
Distinguish Trust Items from Guaranteed Items
The passage contrasts trust items with items that are 'guaranteed in themselves' (madmunah bi-'ayniha), like usurped property (maghsab). These are valid bases for pledge because the debtor is liable for their value.
Also distinguish items guaranteed by something else (like sold goods in seller's hand) which are also invalid for pledge.
4
Apply the Principle to Other Cases
The principle is that the debt must be a guaranteed financial obligation. Consider other transactions: a loan of money is guaranteed, so it can be secured by pledge. A bailment (wadi'ah) is not, so no pledge.
This illustrates the Hanafi fiqh of pledge: the collateral must secure a real, enforceable debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ لَمَّا ذكر ما لا يجوزُ رهنه أراد أن يذكر ما لا يجوز الرهن به ، فقال : (ولا بالأمانات) : كالوديعة ، والمستعار ومال المضاربة، والشركة [٢] قوله : كالوديعة ... الخ ; لأنَّ القبض في «باب الرهن» قبض مضمون، فلا بد من ضمان ثابت ليقع القبضُ مضموناً ، ويتحقق استيفاء الدين منه، وكذلك لا يصح بالأعيان المضمونة بغيرها، كالمبيع في يد البائع ; لأن الضمان ليس بواجب، فإنه إذا هلك العين لم يضمن البائع شيئاً، لكنه يسقط الثمن، وهو حق البائع، فلا يصح لرهن. وإنما سماه مضموناً بغيره باعتبار سقوط الضمان إن لم يقبض وردّه إذا قبض، وإلا فهو ليس بمضمون ; لأنه إذا هلك يهلك ملك البائع ، فلا يجب عليه شيء، كما إذا هلكت الوديعة. فأما الأعيان المضمونة بعينها، وهي أن يكون مضموناً بالمثل أو بالقيمة عند هلاكه العمد مثل : المغصوبة وبدل الخلع والمهر وبدل الصلح الضمان متقرر، فإنَّه وإن كان قائماً وجب تسليمه، وإن كان هالكا تجب قيمته ، فكان رهناً بما هو مضمون فيصح.
Then when he mentioned what is not valid to pledge, he intended to mention what is not valid to pledge with it, so he said: 'And not with trust items (amanat): like deposits (wadi'ah), borrowed items, capital of a mudarabah, and partnership. [2] His statement: 'like deposits... etc.'; because the taking of possession (qabd) in the chapter of pledge is a guaranteed possession (qabd madmun), so there must be an established guarantee (dhaman) for the possession to be guaranteed and for the recovery of the debt to be realized. Similarly, it is not valid with items that are guaranteed by something else, like the sold item in the hand of the seller, because the guarantee is not obligatory; for if the item perishes, the seller does not guarantee anything, but the price drops, which is the right of the seller, so the pledge is not valid. He called it 'guaranteed by something else' considering the dropping of guarantee if he does not take possession and the return if he takes possession; otherwise it is not guaranteed, because if it perishes it perishes as the seller's property, so nothing is obligatory on him, just as if the deposit perishes. As for items that are guaranteed in themselves, meaning they are guaranteed with a like or value upon their destruction due to deliberate act: like the usurped item, the substitute for khul', the mahr, and the substitute for sulh, the guarantee is established; for even if it exists, it must be delivered, and if it perishes, its value is obligatory, so it becomes a pledge with what is guaranteed, so it is valid.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage explains what types of debts can be secured by a pledge. It states that a pledge cannot be taken for a debt that is based on trust (amanah), such as a deposit (wadi'ah), borrowed item (ariyah), capital of a mudarabah or partnership. The reason is that the possession (qabd) in a pledge must be a guaranteed possession (qabd madmun), meaning the creditor must be able to sell the collateral to recover a guaranteed debt. In trust-based transactions, the item is not guaranteed; if it perishes, the possessor does not compensate. The passage contrasts with items that are guaranteed in themselves (مضمونة بعينها), like usurped goods (maghsab), which are guaranteed by the usurper. The ruling is that one can pledge an item to secure a debt that is itself guaranteed (like a debt arising from a guarantee or a usurpation), but not an unguaranteed trust. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that the pledge is only valid for a debt that is established as a financial obligation with a right of recourse.

Key Terms
الأمانات (al-amanat): Trust items like deposits, borrowed items, mudarabah capital, and partnership capital, where the holder is not liable for loss.
قبض مضمون (qabd madmun): A possession that carries liability; the possessor is responsible for the item.
مضمونة بغيرها (madmunah bi-ghayriha): An item whose guarantee is external to itself, like the sold item in the seller's hand (liability depends on the price).
مضمونة بعينها (madmunah bi-'ayniha): An item that is intrinsically guaranteed, like a usurped item, where the possessor must return it or pay its value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because the deposit cannot be sold

Key Takeaway
A pledge can only secure a debt that is a guaranteed financial obligation; trust items (amanat) are not guaranteed, so a pledge for them is invalid.

Pledge for Darak: Contingent Debt Invalid
Ruling with proof: invalidity of pledging for darak

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pledging for darak and explain why a contingent debt cannot serve as the basis for a valid pledge.

Core Idea
A pledge secures an existing debt; darak is contingent, so pledging for it is invalid.

This lesson addresses a specific but important point in the fiqh of pledge (rahn): can a pledge be taken to secure a contingent future liability known as darak (the seller's guarantee against title defects)? The Hanafi school rules that it is invalid. The reasoning is based on the purpose of a pledge: it is a means of recovery (istifa') of a debt that is already due. Since darak may never become due (the title may never be contested), the pledge lacks a valid basis. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains this and also clarifies that it applies whether the pledgor is the buyer, seller, or a third party. We will dissect the legal reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term 'darak'
Understand that darak is the seller's obligation to return the price if the sold property is rightfully claimed by someone else. It is a contingent (future) liability.
It is not an existing debt at the time of sale.
2
State the ruling
The passage rules that a pledge (rahn) for darak is batil (invalid).
The Arabic phrase 'لا يصح الرهن بالدرك' establishes invalidity.
3
Trace the reasoning (talil)
The reason is that a pledge is for recovery (istifa'), and recovery is only possible when the debt is wajib (obligatory). Darak is not yet wajib because the entitlement may not occur.
The phrase 'لأن الرهن للاستيفاء، ولا استيفاء قبل الوجوب' is the core proof.
4
Examine the scope: does the pledgor matter?
The commentator clarifies that it is a mistake to think the ruling only applies when a third party gives the pledge. Even if the seller himself pledges, it remains invalid.
The text explicitly says 'أخذ الرهن من البائع أيضاً باطل'.
5
Distinguish from kafalah
The passage notes that commitments (iltizam) like kafalah can be contingent, but that is a different contract. Rahn requires an existing debt.
This shows the specificity of the ruling to rahn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بالدرك [١] قوله: ولا بالدرك; أي لا يصح الرهن بالدرك; لأن الرهن للاستيفاء، ولا استيفاء قبل الوجوب; لأنَّ معنى الدركِ ضمان الثمن عند استحقاق المبيع، فما لا يستحق لا يجب على البائع ردُّ الثمن، وكذا بعد الاستحقاق حتى يحكم برد الثمن، ويفسخ البيع; لاحتمال أن يجيز المستحق البيع. [٢] قوله: صورته... الخ; أقول: هذا التصوير لوهم أن وضع المسألة على أنّ الراهن هو الثالث الأجنبي دائماً، وليس كذلك; لأنَّ أخذ الرهن من البائع أيضاً باطل، كما صرح به الأكمل لله. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي). [٣] قوله: ولو كفل... الخ; أي بما ذاب له على فلان، فإنّها عقد التزام، والالتزامات مما يصح تعليقها بالأخطار، كإيجاب الصوم والصلاة والصدقة، فإنها تحتمل التعليق بالخطر والإضافة.
And not with darak. [1] His statement: 'and not with darak'; i.e., the pledge with darak is not valid because the pledge is for recovery (istifa'), and there is no recovery before obligation (wujub); because the meaning of darak is the guarantee of the price upon the entitlement (istihqaq) of the sold item. So what is not entitled does not obligate the seller to return the price, and likewise after entitlement until it is judged to return the price and the sale is rescinded, due to the possibility that the entitled party may validate the sale. [2] His statement: 'its image...'; I say: this depiction is due to the mistaken assumption that the case always involves the third party as the pledgor, and it is not so; because taking the pledge from the seller is also invalid, as stated by al-Akmal li'llah. That is in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [3] His statement: 'and if he guarantees...'; i.e., with what is due to him from so-and-so, for it is a contract of commitment (iltizam), and commitments are validly suspended on contingencies, like the obligation of fasting, prayer, and charity, for they admit suspension on contingency and addition.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage states that a pledge cannot be taken to secure 'darak' – which is a guarantee against defects in title. For example, if someone buys a property and later it turns out that someone else has a rightful claim, the seller must return the price. The darak is a contingent future obligation. Since a pledge requires an existing debt (for immediate recovery), and darak is not yet due (it may never be), the pledge is invalid. The commentary also clarifies that it doesn't matter if the pledgor is a third party or the seller; in both cases it is invalid. However, if the obligation becomes certain (e.g., after judgment), some consider it valid. The passage also notes that a pledge for a debt that is already due but under a guarantee (kafalah) may be valid, but that is different.

Key Terms
الدرك (al-darak): Guarantee of title; liability for defects in sold property, specifically the seller's obligation to return the price if the title is defective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is darak in the context of this passage?
A) A loan
B) A guarantee against title defects
C) A type of collateral
D) A penalty

Key Takeaway
A pledge cannot secure a contingent debt; darak (guarantee of title) is contingent, so a pledge for it is invalid in the Hanafi school.

Invalid Basis: Item Guaranteed by Another
Ruling on invalidity of pledging for externally guaranteed debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a debt is intrinsically guaranteed and state the Hanafi ruling that a pledge is batil if the debt is guaranteed by something else.

Core Idea
A pledge is only valid if the debt is intrinsically guaranteed; external guarantee makes the pledge batil.

In Hanafi fiqh, a valid pledge (rahn) requires that the debt being secured be intrinsically guaranteed (مضمونة بعينها). Not all debts qualify; for example, the seller's claim for the price of an undelivered sold item is not intrinsically guaranteed—it is guaranteed by the price. This lesson unpacks this distinction and its consequences for pledge validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is pledging an item to secure a debt that is not intrinsically guaranteed. The ruling in the Hanafi school is batil (invalid).
2
Understand the key distinction
Distinguish between 'مضمونة بعينها' (intrinsically guaranteed) and 'مضمونة بغيرها' (guaranteed by something else). An intrinsically guaranteed debt, like a usurped item, must be returned or paid; an externally guaranteed debt, like the price of an undelivered sold item, relies on an external factor.
Example: Sold item in seller's hand is guaranteed by the price, not by itself.
3
Apply the distinction to the example
If a seller sells an item but does not deliver, the buyer has a claim for the price. The item itself is not directly guaranteed. If it perishes, the seller loses the price but does not 'guarantee' the item. Thus, pledging to secure this debt is invalid.
4
Analyse the rationale (talil)
The pledge is invalid because the debt's guarantee is external. If the pledge perishes, the creditor gets nothing, which is 'اعتبار الباطل' (a consideration of falsehood). This logic establishes the condition.
The condition: the debt must be intrinsically guaranteed.
5
Conclude with the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that only debts that are intrinsically guaranteed (مضمونة بعينها) can be secured by a valid pledge. All other debts, including those based on a sold item in the seller's hand, are invalid bases for rahn.
This is a mufta bihi ruling from the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بعين مضمونة بغيرها [٤] [المراد أن لا تكون مضمونة بالمثل أو بالقيمة: (كمبيع في يد البائع): أي باعَ شيئاً ولم يسلّمه فرهن به شيئاً لا يجوز؛ لأنه إذا هلك العين لم يضمن البائع شيئاً، لكنه يسقط الثمن، وهو حق البائع. [٤] قوله: كمبيع في يد البائع; لأنه مضمون بغيره، والرهن لا يجوز إلا بالأعيان المضمونة بنفسها، ولا يجوز بالأعيان المضمونة بغيرها، فإن هلك الرهن بالمبيع ذهب بغير شيء; لأنه اعتبار الباطل، فلا يجب على المشتري شيء.
And not with an item guaranteed by something else. The intended meaning is that it is not guaranteed with a like or value: (like the sold item in the hand of the seller): i.e., he sold something and did not deliver it, then he pledges something with it; it is not valid because if the item perishes, the seller does not guarantee anything, but the price drops, which is the right of the seller. [4] His statement: 'like the sold item in the hand of the seller'; because it is guaranteed by something else, and a pledge is only valid with items guaranteed in themselves, and not with items guaranteed by something else. For if the pledge perishes with the sold item, it goes without anything, because it is a consideration of falsehood, so nothing is obligatory on the buyer.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage explains that a pledge cannot be taken for a debt that is not intrinsically guaranteed. For example, if a seller sells an item but has not yet delivered it, the buyer has a claim on the seller, but the seller's liability is not a direct guarantee of the specific item; rather, if the item perishes, the seller only loses the right to the price. This is considered a liability 'by something else' (بغيرها), i.e., external to the item itself. A valid pledge requires the debt to be secured by an item that is itself guaranteed (مضمونة بعينها), like a usurped item. So pledging to secure a debt that is merely a personal claim without a specific guarantee is invalid.

Key Terms
مضمونة بغيرها (madmunah bi-ghayriha): An item whose guarantee is external to itself, like the sold item in the seller's hand (liability depends on the price).
مضمونة بعينها (madmunah bi-'ayniha): An item that is intrinsically guaranteed, like a usurped item, where the possessor must return it or pay its value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a pledge invalid when the debt is based on a sold item still in the seller's hand?
A) Because the seller still owns it
B) Because the buyer hasn't paid
C) Because the seller's liability is not a direct guarantee of the item
D) Because the sale is incomplete

Key Takeaway
For a valid pledge (rahn), the debt must be intrinsically guaranteed; if the guarantee is external, the pledge is batil.

Invalid Bases for a Pledge: Non-Financial Obligations
Ruling on invalidity of pledging for personal guarantee, qisas, and pre-emption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which obligations cannot be secured by a pledge (rahn) according to the Hanafi school and explain the underlying principle that a pledge is only valid for monetary debts.

Core Idea
A pledge secures only financial obligations; personal guarantees, retaliation, and pre-emption rights are not monetary and thus invalid as pledge bases.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, we encounter a concise ruling: not every obligation can be secured by a pledge (rahn). The text explicitly lists three invalid bases: kafalah bi al-nafs (guarantee to produce a person), qisas (retaliation for homicide or injury), and shuf'a (right of pre-emption). Understanding why these are invalid is essential for correctly structuring pledges in Islamic finance and legal practice. The commentary hints at two reasons, which we will explore to grasp the foundational principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three invalid bases
Read the Arabic text and locate the three items: الكفالة بالنفس, القصاص (بالنفس وما دونها), and الشفعة. These are the obligations for which a pledge is declared invalid.
The text uses the particle 'لا' for negation, indicating a blanket prohibition.
2
Understand the nature of each obligation
Kafalah bi al-nafs is a personal guarantee to produce a person; qisas is a physical retaliation; shuf'a is a right to pre-empt a sale. None of these are monetary debts.
Distinguish between financial debts (dayn) and non-financial obligations (iltizam ghayr mali).
3
Apply the principle that a pledge secures only financial debts
The Hanafi school stipulates that rahn is a security for a monetary claim. Since these three obligations are not monetary, they cannot serve as the underlying debt for a valid pledge.
This principle is rooted in the purpose of rahn: to ensure repayment or fulfillment of a financial liability.
4
Analyze the commentary's 'two reasons'
The commentary from al-Kifayah states that the pledge is invalid 'for two reasons'. Although cut off, the reasons likely are: (1) the obligation is not a debt (dayn) and (2) the collateral cannot be sold to satisfy a non-monetary claim.
These reasons align with the broader Hanafi doctrine that rahn is a contract of guarantee for property (mal) only.
5
Conclude the ruling
The ruling is that a pledge for any of these three bases is batil (void). The mufta bihi position is clear: such pledges have no legal effect.
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is universally accepted within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage gives the final list of invalid bases for a pledge: kafalah bi al-nafs (guarantee of a person's presence), qisas (retaliation for killing or injury), and shuf'a (right of pre-emption). These are all non-monetary obligations. A pledge is a financial instrument for recovering a debt; these obligations cannot be measured in money or satisfied by sale of collateral. The commentary from al-Kifayah is cut off but says it is invalid for two reasons (likely because the obligation is not financial and cannot be enforced by sale).

Key Terms
الكفالة بالنفس (kafalah bi al-nafs): A guarantee to produce a person's presence, typically in court or for a legal proceeding.
القصاص (qisas): Retaliation for homicide or bodily injury; a personal punitive right.
الشفعة (shuf'a): The right of pre-emption in a property sale; a priority to purchase.

Key Takeaway
A pledge (rahn) in Hanafi fiqh is a financial security; it cannot secure non-monetary obligations like personal guarantees, retaliation, or pre-emption rights.

Invalidity of Pledge for Certain Obligations
Listing cases where pledge is invalid due to lack of guaranteed debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid pledge and apply the Hanafi rule that a pledge is void if it does not secure a guaranteed debt.

Core Idea
A pledge is valid only if it secures a guaranteed debt; personal rights or void contracts do not qualify.

The Hanafi school is precise about the conditions for a valid pledge (rahn). The pledged item must be security for a guaranteed financial obligation (mardhun). This lesson examines several specific cases where the pledge fails because the underlying obligation is either non-financial or not guaranteed. Understanding these exceptions clarifies the general rule and prevents invalid transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the listed invalid pledges
Read through the cases: wages of a wailing woman/singer, a slave who committed an offense or is in debt, a personal guarantee (kafala bil-nafs), retaliation (qisas), and the pre-emption price (shuf'a). Note the common factor.
These are all cases where the underlying obligation is not a guaranteed debt.
2
Extract the common factor: absence of a guaranteed debt
In each case, the obligation for which the pledge is given is not mardhun (guaranteed). For example, the wage from a void contract is not owed; the master is not liable for the slave's acts; personal surety and retaliation are not financial.
The key term is 'dayn mardhun' – a guaranteed debt.
3
Understand the rational proof (dalil aqli)
The author gives two reasons: (1) the subject of the obligation cannot be recovered from the pledge, and (2) the obligation itself is not guaranteed – if the subject is lost, no compensation is due. Therefore, the pledge has no secured value.
This is a rational argument based on the purpose of rahn: to secure repayment.
4
Apply the exception: blood money settlement
If the obligation is a financial substitute (badal sulh) for intentional homicide, it is guaranteed. Hence a pledge for it is valid. Contrast this with accidental killing where the settlement is not guaranteed, so the pledge is void.
The guarantee of the substitute makes the difference.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And with the wages of the wailing woman and the female singer, and with the offending slave or the indebted slave: meaning he guaranteed a man's person and then pledged something for it to secure his surrender; and when retaliation becomes obligatory upon him and he pledges something to prevent him from abstaining from retaliation, it is not permissible. Likewise, if the seller or the buyer pledges something to the pre-emptor to surrender the house by pre-emption, it is not permissible due to the absence of debt in these cases. And with the wages of the wailing woman and the female singer (), and with the offending slave or the indebted slave, it is not guaranteed against the master; if it perishes, nothing is owed by the master. The second: the guaranteed thing is not guaranteed in itself; if it perishes, nothing is owed. Commentary
This passage discusses cases where a pledge (rahn) is invalid because the underlying obligation is not a guaranteed debt. The author lists: wages of a wailing woman or female singer (contracts void due to illegality), a slave who has committed an offense or is in debt (master not liable for them), a personal guarantee (kafala bil-nafs) where the guaranteed person's surrender is not a financial right, retaliation (qisas) which is a personal right, and the price in pre-emption (shuf'a) which is not guaranteed against the buyer. The reasoning is that a pledge requires a secured obligation that is guaranteed (mardhun); if the obligation is not guaranteed, the pledge is void. The author then provides two proofs: (1) the obligated thing cannot be recovered from the pledge, and (2) the obligated thing is not inherently guaranteed. Exception: if the obligation is a financial substitute (badal sulh) for intentional homicide, it is guaranteed, so pledging for it is valid. For accidental killing, if settled for a specific property, that property is not guaranteed (if lost, settlement void), so pledge invalid. The commentary (sharh) clarifies reasons.

مضمون (mardhun): Guaranteed; refers to an obligation that if lost, must be compensated.
كفالة (kafala): Suretyship; guaranteeing a person's presence (kafala bil-nafs) is not a financial right, so pledging for it is invalid.

Pledging Wine and the Condition of Guaranteed Property
Ruling on wine pledge between Muslim and dhimmi with proof from ghasb analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pledging wine between a Muslim and a dhimmi, derive the underlying reason (mal mutaqawwim), and identify the condition that a pledge must secure a guaranteed obligation.

Core Idea
Wine is not property for a Muslim, so pledging it is void; a pledge must be against a guaranteed obligation.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains two interconnected rulings: first, the special case of wine in pledge contracts between Muslims and dhimmis, and second, the general requirement that the secured debt or obligation must be guaranteed by its like or value. Understanding these rules clarifies how the Hanafi school defines property (mal) and the purpose of pledge (rahn). We will unpack the reasoning and apply it to concrete scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the object
Note that the object is wine (khamr) and the parties can be Muslim or dhimmi. The ruling changes depending on which party is the pledgor and which is the pledgee.
2
Apply the concept of mal mutaqawwim
Determine whether wine is considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) for each party. In the Hanafi school, wine is only mal mutaqawwim for dhimmis, not for Muslims.
This is because Muslims are prohibited from using wine, so it has no permissible use and therefore no legal value.
3
Determine liability based on direction of pledge
If a Muslim pledges wine to a dhimmi, the pledge is void; the dhimmi is not liable if the wine is lost. If a dhimmi pledges wine to a Muslim, the pledge is valid and the Muslim pledgee is liable because the wine has value for the dhimmi pledgor.
The reason is that a pledge requires the ability to fulfill (ifa') and recover (istifa'), which a Muslim cannot do with wine.
4
Generalize the principle of guaranteed obligation
The passage also states that a pledge is valid only if it secures a specific property guaranteed by its like or value (ayn mardhuna bi al-mithl aw al-qima). Examples include usurped property, dower, etc.
This condition applies to all pledges: the secured right must be a guaranteed debt or obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا رهن خمر وارتهانها من مسلم أو ذمي للمسلم ، ولا يضمن له مرتهنها ذمياً، وفي عكسه الضمان، وصح بعين مضمونة بالمثل أو بالقيمة ؛ كالمغصوب وبدل الخلع، والمهر، وبدل الصلح عن دم عمد، وبالدين ولو موعوداً، بأن رهن ولا رهن خمر وارتهانها من مسلم أو ذمي للمسلم : أي لا يجوز للمسلم أن يرهن خمرا ويرتهنها من مسلم أو ذمي ، ولا يضمن له مرتهنها ذمياً ، وفي عكسه الضمان : أي إن رهن المسلم من ذمي خمرا فهلكت في يد الذمي لا يضمن للمسلم شيئاً، وإن رهن الذمي من المسلم خمراً فهلكت في يد المسلم للذمي ؛ لأنها مال متقوم في حق الذمي دون المسلم وصح. (وصح بعين مضمونة بالمثل أو بالقيمة ؛ كالمغصوب وبدل الخلع، والمهر، وبدل الصلح عن دم عمد)، فإنّ هذه الأشياء إذا كانت قائمة يجب عينها، وإن هلكت يجب المثل أو القيمة ، فيصح الرهن بها، وبالدين ولو موعوداً، بأن رهن الإجارة على ذلك باطلة، والأجرة غير مضمونة ، والرهن إذا لم يكن في مقابلته شيء مضمون كان باطلاً. كذا في «البيانية».[٢] قوله : أي لا يجوز ... الخ ؛ وذلك لأن الرهن للاستيفاء والإيفاء، والمسلم لا يملك الإيفاء إذا كان هو الراهن، ولا يملك الاستيفاء إذا كان هو المرتهن، وكذا الحال في الخنزير، وقوله : ولا يضمن للمسلم شيئا. كما لا يضمن الذمي لو غصب خمر المسلم ، وقوله : يضمن المسلم للذمي كما يضمنها لو غصبها منه فرهنها بالنسيئة إلى الذمي غير باطل ؛ بناءً على أن الرهن إذا كان في مقابلته شيء مضمون لم يكن باطلاً ، كما فهم من كلام صاحب «البيان»، وإن كان ارتهانها باطلاً بالنسيئة إلى المسلم بناءً على قوله المذكور آنفاً. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي».[٣]
And no pledge of wine and taking it as pledge from a Muslim or a dhimmi for the Muslim, and the dhimmi pledgee is not liable to him for it, and in the opposite case there is liability. And a pledge is valid with a specific property guaranteed by its like or value, such as the usurped (property), the substitute for khul', the dower, the substitute for blood money for intentional homicide, and by debt even if future. That is, it is not permissible for a Muslim to pledge wine or take it as pledge from a Muslim or a dhimmi, and the dhimmi pledgee is not liable to him for it. And in the opposite case there is liability: meaning if a Muslim pledges wine to a dhimmi and it is lost in the dhimmi's hand, the dhimmi is not liable to the Muslim for anything; and if a dhimmi pledges wine to a Muslim and it is lost in the Muslim's hand, the Muslim is liable to the dhimmi, because it is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) in the right of the dhimmi but not for the Muslim, and it is valid. (And a pledge is valid with a specific property guaranteed by its like or value, such as the usurped, the substitute for khul', the dower, the substitute for blood money for intentional homicide); these things if they exist, their exact entity is due, and if they perish, their like or value is due, so pledging with them is valid. And by debt even if future, such that if he pledges... the hire for that is void and the wage is not guaranteed, and if the pledge is not in exchange for something guaranteed, it is void. Thus in al-Bayaniyya. He said: 'that is, it is not permissible...' because the pledge is for recovery and fulfillment, and the Muslim cannot fulfill if he is the pledgor, nor can he recover if he is the pledgee; similarly for swine. And his saying: 'the dhimmi is not liable to the Muslim for anything', just as the dhimmi is not liable if he usurps the Muslim's wine. And his saying: 'the Muslim is liable to the dhimmi', just as he would be liable if he usurped it from him. So if he pledges it by deferred payment to the dhimmi, it is not void, based on the understanding that if the pledge is in exchange for something guaranteed, it is not void, as understood from the author of al-Bayan, even if taking it as pledge by deferred payment to the Muslim is void based on his aforementioned statement. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage covers two topics: first, the ruling on pledging wine (khamr) between Muslims and dhimmis. Since wine is not considered property (mal mutaqawwim) for Muslims, a Muslim cannot validly pledge wine or take it as pledge; if a Muslim pledges wine to a dhimmi, the dhimmi is not liable if it perishes because wine has no value for the Muslim. Conversely, if a dhimmi pledges wine to a Muslim, the Muslim is liable because wine is property for the dhimmi; the pledge is valid in that direction. The reason is that a pledge requires the ability to fulfill (ifa') and recover (istifa'), and a Muslim cannot fulfill wine nor recover it. Second, the passage states that a valid pledge must be against a specific property that is guaranteed by its like or value (ain mardhuna bi al-mithl aw al-qima). Examples include usurped property (maghsub), the substitute in khul', the dower (mahr), and the blood money settlement (badal sulh) for intentional homicide. Debt, even if future (muwa'ad), is also valid. The commentary elaborates that if a pledge secures something not guaranteed, the contract is void.

Key Terms
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Property that is legally recognized and has value; wine is mal mutaqawwim for dhimmis but not for Muslims.
استيفاء (istifa'): Recovery of the secured right from the pledge.
إيفاء (ifa'): Fulfillment of the obligation by the pledgor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between Muslims and dhimmis in pledge contracts involving wine due to the concept of mal mutaqawwim, and further requires that a pledge secure a guaranteed obligation.

Valid Pledge: Guaranteed Property by Like or Value
Stating what types of property can validly be pledged

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for a valid pledge in the Hanafi school and identify which types of property satisfy it.

Core Idea
A pledge is valid only when it secures a guarantee — a debt or property that must be returned by like or value.

The validity of a pledge (rahn) depends not on the pledged item itself, but on the underlying obligation. In this lesson, we unpack the Hanafi condition that the secured right must be 'guaranteed' (mardhun), meaning it is either replaceable by an identical item (mithl) or by its monetary value (qima). The text gives concrete examples from everyday contracts — usurpation, marriage, divorce, and homicide — to illustrate what counts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central condition
Read the opening phrase: 'وصح بعين مضمونة بالمثل أو بالقيمة'. The condition for a valid pledge is that the secured item (the debt or obligation) must be 'mardhun' — legally guaranteed. This means if it is lost, the debtor must repay a like (same type/quantity) or its value.
2
Examine the examples
The text lists: usurped property (maghsub), khul' compensation (badal al-khul'), dower (mahr), blood money settlement (badal al-sulh 'an dam 'amd), and debt (dayn). For each, determine why they satisfy the 'guaranteed' condition.
Think: each of these is a fixed obligation that must be paid or returned.
3
Spot the counterexample
The commentary adds: a pledge securing a void hire contract (ijara batila) is invalid because the wage is not guaranteed. What makes the wage non-guaranteed?
The hire contract is void, so no obligation arises.
4
Formulate the rule
From the examples and counterexample, derive the rule: the secured right must be a guaranteed obligation (mardhun). If it is not — for instance, it is contingent, non-obligatory, or based on a void contract — the pledge is void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح بعين مضمونة بالمثل أو بالقيمة ؛ كالمغصوب وبدل الخلع، والمهر، وبدل الصلح عن دم عمد، وبالدين ولو موعوداً، بأن رهن الإجارة على ذلك باطلة، والأجرة غير مضمونة ، والرهن إذا لم يكن في مقابلته شيء مضمون كان باطلاً. كذا في «البيانية».
And a pledge is valid with a specific property guaranteed by its like or value, such as the usurped (property), the substitute for khul', the dower, the substitute for blood money for intentional homicide, and by debt even if future; that if one pledges... the hire for that is void and the wage is not guaranteed, and if the pledge is not in exchange for something guaranteed, it is void. Thus in al-Bayaniyya.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage states the general principle for a valid pledge (rahn) in the Hanafi school: the pledged item must be a specific property (ayn) that is guaranteed (mardhun) either by its like (mithl) or its value (qima). This means that if the property is lost, the debtor must return the same type or pay its value. Examples given include: usurped property (maghsub) — the usurper must return the exact item or its value; the substitute in khul' (divorce by compensation) — the wife's payment is guaranteed; the dower (mahr) — the husband's obligation to pay is guaranteed; the blood money settlement (badal sulh) for intentional homicide — the payment is guaranteed. Debt, even if it is future (muwa'ad), also qualifies as a valid secured obligation. The commentary adds that if a pledge secures something not guaranteed (like a void hire contract), the pledge is void.

Key Terms
عين مضمونة (ayn mardhuna): A specific property that is legally guaranteed; if lost, the obligation to return it or its value remains.
بدل الخلع (badal al-khul'): Compensation paid by a wife to obtain divorce; it is a guaranteed debt.
دين موعود (dayn muwa'ad): A future debt that is contractually agreed; it becomes guaranteed when due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Usurped property (maghsub)
B) The dower (mahr)
C) Wages from a void hire contract
D) The substitute for blood money (badal sulh)

Key Takeaway
A pledge is valid only when the debt it secures is a guaranteed obligation (mardhun), enforceable by like or value.
