Pawn with Promise to Lend: Ruling on Perishing in Pledgee's Hands
Ruling on pawn perishing after promise to lend

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a pawn given with a promise to lend perishes in the pledgee's hands, and explain the condition that the debt must not exceed the pawn's value.

Core Idea
A promise to lend is legally binding when a pawn is given; if the pawn perishes, the pledgee must still pay the promised amount.

This lesson examines a specific pawn contract where the pledgor gives a pawn with the condition that the pledgee will lend a certain amount. The Hanafi school treats the promise as effective due to necessity, so if the pawn perishes before the loan is given, the pledgee is still liable for the promised amount. We will explore the ruling and its condition regarding the debt's value relative to the pawn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the act: a pawn is given with a promise to lend. When the pawn perishes, the ruling is that the pledgee must deliver the promised amount. This is the core ruling.
2
Understand the rationale
The promise is treated as fulfilled because of need (haja). This is an application of istihsan, as people rely on such promises for lending.
3
Recognize the key condition
The guarantee by the debt applies only when the promised debt is less than or equal to the pawn's value. If the debt exceeds the value, the guarantee is limited to the value.
4
Consider the omitted case
The author did not mention the case of debt exceeding value because it is rare, but its ruling is inferable from the general principle.
5
Relate to broader Hanafi principles
This ruling shows how need can make a promise binding in muamalat, illustrating the Hanafi use of istihsan to fulfill practical needs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليقرضه كذا ، فهلكُهُ في يد المرتهن عليه بما وعده : أي إن هلك في يد المرتهن فللراهن على المرتهن المقدار الذي وَعَدَ إقراضه ، فهلكه : بالرفع مبتدأ، وفي يد المرتهن : صفته، وعليه : خبره ، واعلم أنَّ الرهن إنَّما يكون مضموناً بالدين الموعودِ إذا كان الدين مساوياً للقيمة أو أقلّ، أما إذا كان أكثر فلا يكون مضموناً بالدين بل بالقيمة، وإنما لم يذكر هذا القسم ؛ لأن الظاهر أن لا يكون الدين أكثر من قيمة الرهن، وإن كان على سبيل الندرة فحكمه يعلم مما سبق، فاعتمد على ذلك.
[The matn:] 'He pawned it so that he would lend him such-and-such, then it perished in the pledgee's hand, [and it is] against him for what he promised.' [Commentary:] Meaning, if it perishes in the hand of the pledgee, then the pledgor has a claim upon the pledgee for the amount he promised to lend. 'Fa-hulkuhu' is nominative as the subject, 'fi yadi al-murtahin' is its description, and 'alayhi' is its predicate. Know that the pawn is only guaranteed by the promised debt when the debt equals the value or is less. As for when it is more, it is not guaranteed by the debt but by the value. He did not mention this division because the apparent situation is that the debt is not greater than the value of the pawn; even if it occurs rarely, its ruling is known from what preceded. So rely on that.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn with commentary expansion. It addresses a specific scenario: a person gives a pawn with a promise that the pledgee will lend him a certain amount. If the pawn perishes in the pledgee's possession before the loan is given, the pledgee must still deliver the promised amount because the promise is treated as fulfilled due to necessity (haja). The commentator clarifies that the pawn is only guaranteed by the debt (i.e., the pledgee owes the debt) if the debt is less than or equal to the pawn's value; if the debt exceeds the value, the guarantee is limited to the value of the pawn. The author omitted this latter case because it is rare, but its ruling is inferable from the general principles. The underlying logic is that people need loans, and lenders require security, so the promise is legally considered as existing to facilitate the transaction.

Key Terms
الموعود (al-maw'ud): The amount promised to be lent, which is treated legally as existing even though not yet given.
مضموناً بالدين (madmunan bid-dayn): The pawn is guaranteed against the debt, meaning the pledgee is liable for the debt if the pawn perishes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The pledgor loses his pawn and gets nothing.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats a promise to lend made in conjunction with a pawn as binding; if the pawn perishes, the pledgee must pay the promised amount (up to the pawn's value).

Validity of Pawning for Salam Capital and Currency Price
Ruling on validity of pawning for salam capital and currency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on pawning for salam capital and currency price, apply the consequences when the pawn perishes or the parties separate, and distinguish the special rule for the musallam fihi.

Core Idea
Pawning for salam capital is valid as security, not a sale; perishing in session counts as collection, but separation without payment voids the contract.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of pawning for the capital of a salam contract or the price in a currency exchange (sarf). While Zufar argues that such a pawn implies an impermissible substitution (istibdal), the majority holds that it is a valid security (istithaq) with distinct legal consequences. We will walk through the dispute, the proofs, and the detailed scenarios that arise when the pawn perishes or the parties separate.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and the majority) holds pawning for salam capital and currency price to be valid (sahih). The passage states: 'يَجوزُ الرهن بهذه الأشياء.' (It is permissible to pawn for these things.)
2
Understand Zufar's objection
Zufar argues it is invalid because it leads to istifa' (taking possession) through a forbidden substitution (istibdal) in contracts where immediate exchange is required. Identify his reasoning: 'لأنَّ حكمه الاستيفاء، وذلك بالاستبدال، والاستبدال حرام في بدل الصرف والسلم.'
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes istithaq (seeking security) from istifa'. The pledgee only becomes entitled to the value (maliyyah), not the specific item. The pawn remains a trust (amanah) in the pledgee's hands. Thus, no forbidden exchange occurs. Recognize the key phrase: 'إنه استيثاق من الوجه الذي عني، وهو المقصود بالرهن.'
4
Apply the scenarios: perishing and separation
Two scenarios are given for a pawn given for salam capital or currency price: (1) If the pawn perishes in the session before separation, the pledgee is considered to have collected his right (istawfa). (2) If the parties separate without paying and the pawn perishes, the underlying contract (salam or sarf) becomes void (batal). Trace the logic: perishing in session fulfills the condition of possession; separation before payment fails the condition.
5
Distinguish the musallam fihi pawn
The passage notes that the previous detail does not apply to a pawn given for the musallam fihi (the commodity to be delivered in salam). Such a pawn is valid absolutely and does not become void upon separation. If it perishes, the pledgee becomes entitled to the value. Additionally, if the salam contract is rescinded, the pawn stands for the return of capital. Understand why: possession of the musallam fihi is not required in the session.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبرأس مال السلم وثمن الصرف والمسلّم فيه : أي يجوزُ الرهن بهذه الأشياء، وقال زفر : لا يجوز ؛ لأنَّ حكمه الاستيفاء، وذلك بالاستبدال ، والاستبدال حرام في بدل الصرف والسلم. ولنا : إنه استيثاق من الوجه الذي عنى، وهو المقصود بالرهن، وإنما يصير مستوفياً بالمالية لا بالعين ؛ ولهذا يكون عينه أمانةً في يده ، حتى نفقته حياً وكفنه ميتاً على الراهن، ولو كان مستوفياً به لوجب على المرتهن، وهما من حيث المالية جنس واحد، فيجوز استيفاء لا مبادلة.
فإن هلك في المجلس فقد أخذ، وإن افترقا قبل نقدِ وهَلَكَ بطلا ، ورهنُ المُسلّم فيه رهن ببدله إذا فسخ : أي إذا رهن برأس مال السلم أو ثمن الصرف، فإن هلك الرهن قبل الافتراق فالمرتهن قد استوفى حقه، وإن افترقا قبل نقدِ المرهون به وقبل هلاكِ المرهون بطل السلم والصرف، وهذا التفصيلُ لا يتأتى في الرهن بالمسلّم فيه، فيصح مطلقاً، فإن هلك الرهن يصير مستوفياً للمسلم فيه، فلا يبقى السلم.

[The matn:] 'And for the capital of salam, the price of currency exchange, and the musallam fihi.' [Commentary:] Meaning, it is permissible to pawn for these things. Zufar said: It is not permissible because its ruling is istifa' (taking possession), and that is by substitution, and substitution is forbidden in the exchange of currency and salam. Our proof: It is istithaq (seeking security) from the intended perspective, which is the purpose of pawning. He only becomes entitled to possession in terms of value, not the specific item; therefore, the item itself is a trust in his hand, so its maintenance when alive and its shroud when dead are upon the pledgor. If he were taking possession through it, it would be obligatory upon the pledgee. Both are of one genus in terms of value, so it is permissible to take possession without exchange.
[The matn:] 'If it perishes in the session, he has taken; if they separate before payment and it perishes, both become void. And the pawn of the musallam fihi is a pawn for its substitute if they rescind.' [Commentary:] Meaning, if he pawns for the capital of salam or the price of currency, if the pawn perishes before separation, the pledgee has collected his right; if they separate before paying the one for which the pawn was given and before the pawn perishes, the salam and exchange become void. This detail does not apply to the pawn for the musallam fihi; it is valid absolutely. If the pawn perishes, he becomes entitled to the musallam fihi, so the salam does not remain.
[The matn:] 'And the pawn of the musallam fihi is a pawn for its substitute if they rescind.' [Commentary:] Meaning, if an item is pawned for the musallam fihi, then they rescind the salam contract, it becomes a pawn for the substitute: i.e., the salam investor may hold the pawn until he receives the capital.
[Further commentary:] 'His saying: “He has taken”—etc.'; due to the realization of possession and the unity of type in terms of value, which is the guaranteed thing. This is if the pawn perishes before separation. If they separate before perishing, the exchange and salam become void due to loss of possession in reality and legally. This is if the pawn was for the substitute of currency or capital of salam. If the pawn was for the musallam fihi, it does not become void by separation. As the commentator said: 'This detail—etc.'; because its possession is not required in the session. Then if it perishes before separation, he becomes entitled to his debt legally, so the salam is complete, just as if it were a pawn for capital or currency substitute and perished before separation, he becomes entitled to his debt. His saying: 'The salam and exchange become void' is due to the loss of the condition of their validity, which is possession in the session. As for loss in reality, it is apparent; as for legally, because the pledgee only becomes possessor after perishing, which is after separation. Al-Jalabi said.

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the validity of pawning for the capital of salam (advance payment for future goods) and the price of currency exchange (sarf). The matn declares it permissible. Zufar objects, arguing that such a pawn implies taking possession (istifa') through an impermissible exchange (istibdal) in these contracts. The Hanafi response clarifies that the pawn is actually a form of seeking security (istithaq), not taking possession of the specific item; the pledgee's right is only in value, and the item itself remains a trust. The commentary then details the consequences: if the pawn perishes in the session before separation, the pledgee is considered to have collected his right; if they separate before payment and the pawn perishes, the underlying contract (salam or sarf) becomes void. However, for a pawn given for the musallam fihi (the commodity due in a salam contract), the ruling is different: it is valid absolutely, and perishing makes the pledgee entitled to the commodity. Additionally, if the salam contract is rescinded, the pawn stands for the capital return. The commentary explains that the condition of possession in the session applies only to salam and sarf, not to the musallam fihi pawn because its possession is not required in the session.

Key Terms
استيفاء (istifa'): Taking possession of one's right, here meaning directly receiving the item owed.
استيثاق (istithaq): Seeking security or assurance, the primary objective of a pawn transaction.
مسلم فيه (musallam fihi): The commodity to be delivered in a salam contract; the pawn for it has special rules.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates pawning for salam capital and currency price as a security, with specific consequences for perishing and separation that distinguish it from a direct sale.

Pledge for Salam/Sarf Capital: Effect of Destruction and Separation
Ruling on pledge for salam/sarf capital with destruction and separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a pledge for the capital of a salam or the substitute in sarf is destroyed or the parties separate before payment, and explain why the contract becomes void upon separation.

Core Idea
Destruction before separation perfects possession; separation before destruction voids the contract.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on pledges made to secure the capital of a salam (forward sale) or the substitute in a sarf (currency exchange) contract. The key condition is immediate possession (qabd) in the session. The passage distinguishes between destruction of the pledge before the parties separate (which perfects possession legally) and separation before destruction (which voids the contract because the condition is lost).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The subject is a pledge for the capital of salam or the substitute in sarf. Determine the ruling when the pledge is destroyed before separation versus when the parties separate before destruction.
2
Locate the condition
The condition for validity of salam and sarf is immediate possession (qabd) in the session. Identify how the passage uses this condition.
Qabd is a condition of validity (shart sihha) for these contracts.
3
Understand the effect of destruction
If the pledge is destroyed before the parties separate, the pledgee is deemed to have taken possession legally, so the contract stands.
Destruction compensates for lack of actual possession.
4
Understand the effect of separation
If the parties separate before destruction, the condition of qabd is lost both actually and legally, so the contract becomes void (batil).
Actual loss: no physical possession; legal loss: no deemed possession via destruction.
5
Contrast with pledge for muslam fihi
The passage notes that this detail does not apply to a pledge for the muslam fihi (subject matter of salam), which is valid absolutely. This lesson focuses only on the capital/substitute pledge.
The distinction is important; muslam fihi is a debt, not a substitute.

[1] His statement: 'he has taken possession, etc.' because possession is realized, and the type is unified in terms of monetary value, and it is the guaranteed thing. This is if the pledge is destroyed before separation. If they separate before destruction, the sarf and salam are void due to the loss of possession actually and legally. This is if it is a pledge for the substitute of sarf or for the capital of salam. But if it is a pledge for the muslam fihi, it is not void by separation. As the commentator said: 'And this detail does not apply...' because its possession is not obligatory in the session; then if it is destroyed before separation, he becomes taking possession of his debt legally, so the salam is completed, just as if it were a pledge for the capital or the substitute of sarf, destroyed before separation, he becomes taking possession of his debt. [2] His statement: 'the salam and sarf become void' due to the loss of a condition of their validity, which is possession in the session. As for actual loss, it is obvious; as for legal loss, because the pledgee only becomes a possessor after destruction, which is after separation. Al-Jalabi said... [3] His statement: 'it is a pledge for the substitute' by istihsan; because it is its substitute, so it stands in its place, and it becomes like the usurped object if it is destroyed, and by it a pledge, is a pledge for its value. So the salam principal has the right to detain the pledge for receiving the capital, even though it is not a pledge for it, because in the pledge for the muslam fihi there is receiving of it from one aspect. So it becomes as if he actually received it; and if he actually received it and then they rescinded, he would have the right to detain it. (From Dhakhirat al-Uqba p.596). If his pledge is destroyed after rescission, it is destroyed with it. And for a debt on him is a slave of his child... Explanation: If the muslam ilayh pledges something with the salam principal for the muslam fihi, then they rescind the salam contract and the pledge is destroyed in the hand of the salam principal, its destruction is for the muslam fihi: meaning the salam principal must give to the muslam ilayh the amount of food that was the muslam fihi; because when the pledge is destroyed, it is as if the salam principal received the right of the muslam fihi; because the hand of the pledgee on the pledge is a hand of receiving, so it becomes confirmed by destruction, so it is as if the salam principal received the muslam fihi, then they rescinded the contract, so the salam principal must pay the muslam fihi to the muslam ilayh. [1] His statement: 'destroyed with it, etc.' So the pledgee must give the like of the food that was due to him from the muslam ilayh, and take his capital; because by taking possession of the money, its monetary value became guaranteed by the food of the salam, and the ruling of the pledge remained until it was destroyed, so by its destruction the pledger becomes having received the food of the salam. And he actually received it before the rescission, then they rescinded, he is obliged to return the received for equivalence of capital. So also here; because rescission in salam does not admit of annulment after it is established. So the destruction of the pledge does not void the rescission; it is only made destroyed by the food, not by the capital, because it is not a pledge for it, but it is detained for it for what we said. It is permissible that a thing be detained for a thing without being guaranteed by it. So in Ghayat al-Bayan.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary on al-Hidayah, explaining the rulings regarding a pledge made for the capital of a salam contract or the substitute in a sarf contract. The key issues are the effects of destruction (halak) of the pledge and separation (iftiraq) of the parties before payment. The author distinguishes between a pledge for the capital/substitute and a pledge for the muslam fihi (the subject of salam). In the first case, if the pledge is destroyed before the parties separate, the pledgee is deemed to have received his right, and the contract is valid. If they separate before destruction, the contract is void because the condition of immediate possession (qabd) is lost both actually and legally. In the second case, if the pledge is for the muslam fihi (which is a debt), the contract is valid absolutely, and if the pledge is destroyed, the pledgee is considered to have received the muslam fihi. Additionally, if the parties rescind the salam contract, the pledge for the muslam fihi becomes a pledge for its substitute (the return of the capital), allowing the pledgee to hold the pledge until he receives the capital. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) because the substitute takes the place of the original. The commentary elaborates that the pledgee's hand is a hand of receiving (yad istifa'), so destruction confirms the receipt. This analysis follows the Hanafi school, relying on the authoritative transmission of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Key Terms
سلم (salam): A forward sale where payment is made in advance for future delivery of specified goods.
صرف (sarf): Currency exchange, specifically the exchange of gold for silver or vice versa.
قبض (qabd): Taking physical or legal possession.
رهن (rahn): A pledge or collateral given to secure a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a salam contract if the parties separate before the pledge for the capital is destroyed?
A) The contract is valid, and the destruction later perfects possession.
B) The contract becomes void due to loss of the condition of immediate possession.
C) The contract is suspended until the pledge is replaced.
D) The contract is valid because the pledge can be destroyed later.

Key Takeaway
For a pledge securing the capital of salam or substitute of sarf, the contract is valid only if the pledge is destroyed before the parties separate; separation before destruction voids the contract.

Pledge for Muslam Fihi: Validity and Rescission Effects
Ruling on pledge for muslam fihi with rescission and destruction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a pledge for the muslam fihi (subject matter of salam) is valid absolutely, and apply the ruling on its transformation into a pledge for the substitute upon rescission.

Core Idea
Pledge for muslam fihi is valid without immediate possession; upon rescission it becomes a pledge for the substitute.

This lesson covers the Hanafi ruling on a pledge given specifically for the muslam fihi (the goods to be delivered in a salam contract). Unlike a pledge for the capital, no immediate possession is required, so it is valid absolutely. Additionally, if the parties rescind the salam contract, this pledge transforms into a pledge for the substitute (the recovery of the capital), based on istihsan. The passage also addresses what happens if the pledge is destroyed after rescission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of pledge
The pledge is given to secure the muslam fihi (the subject matter of salam), not the capital. This is a distinct case from the previous lesson.
Muslam fihi is a debt (dayn) in the Salam contract.
2
Understand the absolute validity
The text states that the detail of requiring immediate possession does not apply here; the pledge is valid even without qabd in the session.
Cause: possession of the muslam fihi is not obligatory in the session.
3
Analyze the effect of destruction before separation
If the pledge is destroyed before separation, the pledgee is deemed to have received the muslam fihi legally, completing the salam contract.
Similar to the capital case, but here the contract is already valid.
4
Understand the transformation upon rescission
If the parties rescind the salam contract, the pledge is now considered a pledge for the substitute (the return of the capital), based on istihsan.
The substitute takes the place of the original, as the usurped object analogy.
5
Examine destruction after rescission
If the pledge is destroyed after rescission, the pledgee (salam principal) is considered to have received the muslam fihi through the destruction, so he must return its equivalent (like of the food) and can take his capital.
The hand of the pledgee is a hand of receiving (yad istifa'), so destruction confirms receipt.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary on al-Hidayah, explaining the rulings regarding a pledge made for the capital of a salam contract or the substitute in a sarf contract. The key issues are the effects of destruction (halak) of the pledge and separation (iftiraq) of the parties before payment. The author distinguishes between a pledge for the capital/substitute and a pledge for the muslam fihi (the subject of salam). In the first case, if the pledge is destroyed before the parties separate, the pledgee is deemed to have received his right, and the contract is valid. If they separate before destruction, the contract is void because the condition of immediate possession (qabd) is lost both actually and legally. In the second case, if the pledge is for the muslam fihi (which is a debt), the contract is valid absolutely, and if the pledge is destroyed, the pledgee is considered to have received the muslam fihi. Additionally, if the parties rescind the salam contract, the pledge for the muslam fihi becomes a pledge for its substitute (the return of the capital), allowing the pledgee to hold the pledge until he receives the capital. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) because the substitute takes the place of the original. The commentary elaborates that the pledgee's hand is a hand of receiving (yad istifa'), so destruction confirms the receipt. This analysis follows the Hanafi school, relying on the authoritative transmission of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Key Terms
مسلم فيه (muslam fihi): The subject matter of a salam contract; the goods to be delivered in the future.
بدل (badal): Substitute; here the capital amount that must be returned upon rescission.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or cancellation of a contract.
استيفاء (istifa'): Receiving satisfaction of a debt or right.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference, a method of reasoning that departs from strict analogy for a more suitable ruling.

Father's Pledge of Minor Child's Slave: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on father pledging minor child's slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on a father pledging his minor child's slave for his own debt and distinguish the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A father can pledge his minor child's slave for his own debt – Abu Hanifa's view prevails despite objections from Abu Yusuf and Zufar.

This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: may a father pledge a slave owned by his minor child as collateral for his own debt? The ruling touches on the scope of paternal guardianship over a child's property. We will explore the positions, the reasoning, and the operative ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
Recognize that the act is a father pledging (rahn) a slave owned by his minor child to secure a debt the father owes.
The slave is property of the child, not the father.
2
State the ruling of each Hanafi authority
Identify that Abu Hanifa holds it valid; Abu Yusuf and Zufar hold it invalid.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze the reasoning for validity
Examine the talil: the father's pledge is for receiving what corresponds to the debt, achieving equality in rescission, and is akin to a contract.
The father's authority over the child's property includes such transactions.
4
Note the restriction to a minor child
Understand that al-Hidayah restricts the ruling to a 'small' (minor) child, excluding an adult child where paternal authority is weaker.
The musannif used 'tifl' absolutely, but al-Hidayah specifies 'saghir'.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Recognize that the relied-upon position for fatwa is Abu Hanifa's view, as indicated by 'هذا عندنا'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And for a debt on him is a slave of his child: it is a conjunction to [the statement about] capital: meaning the pledge of a slave of his child for a debt on the father is valid. This is our [position], and according to Abu Yusuf and Zufar, it is not valid. And it is for receiving what corresponds to it, actualizing equality in rescission which is in the meaning of the contract. So also when he becomes receiving in one aspect. Thus in the commentaries of al-Hidayah. [2] His statement: 'a slave of his child, etc.'; the author (musannif) made it absolute (child), and the author of al-Hidayah restricted it to the minor, where he said: It is permissible for the father to pledge a slave owned by his minor son for a debt on him. And the author of al-Inayah explained that minor is a qualification to exclude the adult, for it is not permissible for the father to ...
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling and an intra-Hanafi dispute. The issue is whether a father can validly pledge a slave owned by his minor child for a debt that the father owes. According to Imam Abu Hanifa (our school), this is valid. However, Abu Yusuf and Zufar disagree and hold that such a pledge is not valid. The reason for validity is that the father has the right to use the child's property for debts that benefit the child or for necessities, and here the pledge is like receiving what corresponds to the debt, achieving equality in rescission. The opposing view likely relies on the principle that the father cannot encumber the child's property without direct need. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the view of Abu Hanifa, as indicated by 'هذا عندنا' (this is our position). The commentator also notes that al-Hidayah restricts the ruling to a minor child, excluding an adult child where the father's authority is less.

Key Terms
عبد (abd): A slave owned by the minor child.
طفل (tifl): A minor child (here specifically small child in al-Hidayah).
أب (ab): Father, as guardian of his minor child's property.
رهن (rahn): Pledge, collateral.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They agree with Abu Hanifa that it is valid.
B) They hold it is invalid.
C) They hold it is valid only if the child is an adult.
D) They hold it is valid only if the debt is for the child's benefit.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that a father may validly pledge his minor child's slave for his own debt, despite the objections of Abu Yusuf and Zufar.

Pledging a Minor's Slave for the Father's Debt: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of a father pledging his minor child's slave for his own debt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a father pledging his minor child's slave for his own debt, identify the intra-Hanafi dispute, and explain the basis of Abu Hanifa's position via istihsan.

Core Idea
A father may pledge his minor child's slave for his own debt, but not an adult's without permission.

The issue of a father using his child's property to secure his own debt touches on guardianship and the limits of paternal authority. In this passage, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute where Abu Hanifa permits the pledge for a minor child, while Abu Yusuf and Zufar disagree. Understanding the reasoning involves the principle of istihsan and the distinction between minor and adult children.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The matn states that the pledge of a child's slave for the father's debt is valid. According to Abu Hanifa, the hukm is sahih (valid).
2
Locate the ikhtilaf
Which Hanafi authorities differ? Abu Yusuf and Zufar hold it invalid. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
The text says 'this according to us, but according to Abu Yusuf and Zufar it is not valid.'
3
Understand the condition
The child must be a minor; the ruling does not extend to adult children without their permission. The author of al-Hidayah explicitly restricts the ruling to minor children.
The original matn's absolute wording 'child' is interpreted as 'minor' by later commentators.
4
Trace the basis
The validity is based on istihsan: the father's right to use the minor's property for necessities, and the child's benefit from the father's financial stability. The text mentions 'achieving equality in rescission' and that the father becomes a 'collector in a sense'.
The istihsan overrides the general rule that one cannot dispose of another's property without permission.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text indicates that Abu Hanifa's view is the relied-upon position ('this according to us'). Later Hanafi works adopt this as the mufta bihi.
The commentary supports this by restricting the ruling to minors, which is consistent with Abu Hanifa's reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبدين عليه عبد طفله : عطف على رأس المال : أي صح الرهن بدين على الأب عبد طفله ، هذا عندنا ، وعند أبي يوسف له وزفر ه لا يصح، وهو لاستيفاء ما يقابله تحقيقاً للمساواة في الإقالة التي هو في معنى العقد، فكذا إذا صار مستوفياً من وجه. كذا في شروح «الهداية». And by a debt on him, the slave of his child: it is connected to the principal: i.e., the pledge is valid for a debt owed by the father of the child's slave. This is according to us, but according to Abu Yusuf and Zufar, it is not valid. And it is for the collection of what corresponds to it to achieve equality in rescission which is like a contract, so likewise when he becomes a collector in a sense. As in the commentaries of al-Hidayah. [2] His statement: 'slave of his child'... etc.; the author mentioned child absolutely, but the author of al-Hidayah restricted it to the minor, where he said: It is permissible for a father to pledge a slave belonging to his minor son for a debt owed by him. And al-Ayni justified it by saying that minor is a precaution against the adult, for it is not permissible for a father to pledge his adult son's slave for his own debt without permission. Perhaps the author intended the restricted by the unrestricted, and this is supported by the commentator's statement in explaining the aspect of istihsan where he said: The aspect of istihsan is that in the reality of payment, it would remove the property of the minor without compensation... etc., so reflect.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a father pledging a slave belonging to his child (his son's slave) as security for his own debt. The matn states that such a pledge is valid. The commentary identifies that this is the position of Abu Hanifa ('according to us'), while Abu Yusuf and Zufar hold that it is not valid. The reasoning for validity is based on istihsan: since the father is allowed to use his minor child's property for his own benefit in certain cases, and the pledge is for the benefit of the child as well? Actually the text says it is for achieving equality in rescission, meaning that by pledging, the father is effectively using the child's property to secure the debt, which is allowed because the child benefits from the father's financial health. The footnote [2] further clarifies that the child must be a minor; an adult child's slave cannot be pledged without permission. The commentator notes that the author's absolute mention of 'child' is restricted to minors by the author of al-Hidayah. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa) is mufta bihi in this context.

Key Terms
طفل (tifl): In this context, it refers to a minor child whose property is under the father's guardianship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The pledge of a minor child's slave for the father's debt is valid under Abu Hanifa's view, which is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.

Pawning with the Price of a Defective Item: Guarantee of the Pledge
Ruling and proof of guarantee when pawning with price of item later found defective

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the guarantee of a pawn when the underlying debt is later found invalid and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas in this case.

Core Idea
A pawn is guaranteed if the debt was outwardly valid, even if the item sold is later defective.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in the law of pawn (rahn): you buy something, pawn something else with the price, and then discover the purchased item was defective (e.g., the slave was free). At first glance, the debt becomes void, so why should the pawn be guaranteed? The Hanafi school introduces the principle of istihsan (juristic preference) to preserve the guarantee. Let's analyze the reasoning through qiyas and istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and the Ruling
The text gives three examples: buying a slave (who appears free), vinegar (which turns out to be wine), or a slaughtered animal (which is dead). In each case, the buyer had pawned something with the price. The ruling is that the pawn is guaranteed (madhmun) – i.e., if it perishes, the pawnbroker must compensate.
The debt is invalid after the defect appears, but the guarantee applies because at pawning it was outwardly valid.
2
Understand the Qiyas (Analogy) Argument
A qiyas would compare this to a father paying his personal debt from his minor child's property. That is not allowed because it removes the child's ownership without compensation. Similarly, pawning with a price that later proves invalid might seem like removing the pawnor's ownership without valid debt.
The qiyas would suggest the pawn is not guaranteed, but Hanafis reject this via istihsan.
3
Apply the Istihsan (Preference) Argument
Istihsan distinguishes: in pawning, ownership of the pawn remains with the pledgor; the pawnbroker is only a custodian. So the analogy to outright removal of ownership fails. The debt was wajib (obligatory) outwardly at the time of contract, so the pawn is valid and guaranteed.
Istihsan here preserves the security interest because the pawn is a 'preserver' of the property, not a transfer of ownership.
4
Determine the Consequence
If the pawn perishes while with the pawnbroker, he must compensate: the full debt amount if the pawn's value equals or exceeds the debt; otherwise, the actual value of the pawn. This is because the pawn was madhmun (guaranteed) from the moment of contract.
The compensation is wajib upon the pawnbroker.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبثمن عبد أو خل أو ذكية إن ظهر العبد حراً، والخل خمراً، والذكية ميتة (1) القياس اعتباراً بحقيقة الإيفاء، وجه الاستحسان : أنَّ في حقيقة الإيفاء إزالة ملكِ الصغير بلا عوض في الحال، وفي هذا نصب حافظ لماله مع بقاءِ ملكه (وبثمن عبدٍ أو خل أو ذكية إن ظهر العبد حراً، والخل خمراً، والذكية ميتة) : أي اشترى عبداً أو خلاً أو شاة مذبوحة، ورهن بثمن المشترى، وهو عشرة دراهم مثلاً شيئاً، ثم ظهر العبد حراً، والخل خمرا، والشاة ميتة فالرهن مضمون ؛ أي إن هلك وقيمته عشرة دراهم أو أكثر فعلى المرتهن عشرة دراهم يؤديها إلى الراهن، وإن كانت قيمته أقل فعليه القيمة ; لأنَّ رهنه بدين واجب ظاهراً. [١]قوله : بحقيقة الإيفاء ; يعني ليس للأب أن يؤدّي دينه من الصغير، فكذا ليس له أن يدفع مال الصغير بجهة الإيفاء ; لأنَّ الرهن محبوس بجهة الإيفاء. [٢] قوله : بدين واجب ظاهراً ; ألا ترى أنَّ البائع والمشتري لو اختصما إلى القاضي قبل ظهور الحرية والاستحقاق فالقاضي يقضي بالثمن، ووجوب الدين ظاهراً يكفي لصحة الرهن، ويصير وزنه مضموناً. كذا في «العناية». [۳] يرهن عبده بدين نفسه إلا بالإذن، فلعل المصنف له أراد المقيد بالمطلق، ويؤيد ذلك قول الشارح له في بيان وجه الاستحسان حيث قال : وجه الاستحسان أنّ في حقيقة الإيفاء إزالة ملك الصغير بلا عوض... الخ، فليتأمل.
And with the price of a slave, or vinegar, or a slaughtered animal: if the slave appears free, the vinegar wine, and the slaughtered animal dead. (1) Analogy considers it according to the reality of repayment; the basis of istihsan is that in the reality of repayment there is removal of the minor's ownership without compensation immediately, while here there is appointing a preserver for his property with continuation of his ownership. (And with the price of a slave, or vinegar, or a slaughtered animal if the slave appears free, the vinegar wine, and the slaughtered animal dead): meaning he bought a slave, or vinegar, or a slaughtered sheep, and pawned something with the price of the purchase, which is ten dirhams for example, then the slave appears free, the vinegar wine, and the sheep dead – so the pledge is guaranteed. That is, if it perishes and its value is ten dirhams or more, then upon the pawnbroker is ten dirhams which he pays to the pledgor; and if its value is less, then upon him is the value, because his pawning was for a debt that was obligatory outwardly. Footnote [1]: His saying: by the reality of repayment – meaning that the father may not pay his debt from the minor's property, so likewise he may not dispose of the minor's property by way of repayment, because the pledge is detained by way of repayment. Footnote [2]: His saying: for a debt obligatory outwardly – do you not see that if the seller and buyer dispute before the judge before the appearance of freedom and entitlement, the judge would rule for the price, and the outward obligation of the debt is sufficient for the validity of the pledge, and its weight becomes guaranteed. As in al-'Inayah. Footnote [3]: He pawns his slave for his own debt except with permission; perhaps the author meant the restricted by the absolute, and this is supported by the commentator's statement in explaining the basis of istihsan: that in the reality of repayment is removal of the minor's ownership without compensation... etc. So ponder.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with its proof regarding pawning with the price of an item that later turns out to be defective. The ruling states that if someone buys a slave, vinegar (presumably for making wine), or a slaughtered animal, and pawns something with its price, and then the slave is discovered to be free, the vinegar to be wine, or the animal to be dead (i.e., the sale is invalid because the item was not owned by the seller or was impure), the pawned item becomes guaranteed (madhmun). This means that if the pawned item perishes while with the pawnbroker (creditor), the pawnbroker must compensate the pledgor: either the full debt amount (if the pawn's value equals or exceeds the debt) or the actual value of the pawn (if less). The reasoning is that at the time of pawning, the debt was apparently valid (wajib zahiran), so the pawn was valid and the pawnbroker is liable for its safekeeping. The passage then discusses a qiyas (analogy) that would consider this as if the pawnbroker had received repayment from the minor's property? Actually the text uses the example of a father paying his own debt from his minor child's property, which is not allowed. The istihsan (preference) argument is that the father is not removing the child's ownership without compensation; rather, he is appointing a preserver of the property while ownership remains. The footnotes clarify: the first footnote explains that 'by the reality of repayment' means the father cannot use the minor's property to pay his own debt, so he also cannot pawn it for repayment because a pawn is held for repayment. The second footnote justifies the outward obligation: if the parties went to court before the defect appeared, the judge would enforce the price, so the debt was outwardly valid, sufficient for a valid pledge. The third footnote addresses a related issue: a father pawning his own slave for his own debt without permission, and suggests that the author may have intended the absolute rule with a restriction.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A pawn/pledge: a property given as security for a debt, held by the creditor.
مضمون (madhmun): Guaranteed: the pawnbroker is liable for the pawn if it perishes, and must compensate the pledgor.
استحسان (istihsan): Preference: a method of juristic preference used to override a qiyas for a stronger reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The pawn is void and must be returned immediately.
B) The pawn is guaranteed, and if it perishes, the pawnbroker must compensate the pledgor.
C) The pawn is a trust (amanah) and not guaranteed.
D) The pawn remains valid only if the pawnbroker is unaware.

Key Takeaway
A pawn is guaranteed if the debt was outwardly valid at the time of contract, even if the underlying sale is later voided.

Guarantee of Pawn Given for Compromise on Denial Despite Later Admission of No Debt
Ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on pawn guarantee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mainstream Hanafi position on the guarantee of a pawn given for compromise on denial when both parties later admit no debt, and explain the counter-argument of Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Outward contract validity creates guarantee; later admission of no debt does not retroactively void it.

This passage addresses a nuanced scenario in pawn (rahn) law: a pawn is given as security for a compromise amount (badal sulh) on denial of debt. After the pawn perishes, both parties agree there was no debt at all. The mainstream Hanafi ruling holds the pawn is still guaranteed (madhmun), meaning the pledgee must compensate. However, Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that their agreement nullifies the basis for guarantee. This intra-Hanafi dispute highlights the tension between outward contractual form and substantive truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
Read the scenario: a claimant demands 1000 dirhams, defendant denies, they compromise on 500 dirhams on denial, and the defendant gives a pawn worth 500. The pawn perishes, then both admit no debt. The text states: 'fa al-rahn madhmun' (the pawn is guaranteed). Identify this as the mainstream position.
The ruling is that the pawn is guaranteed regardless of later admission.
2
Locate the dispute and its basis
The text notes that Abu Yusuf disagrees. The basis: if both admit no debt, they agree on absence of guarantee, and their agreement is binding. Retention without debt is inconceivable.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi: mainstream vs. Abu Yusuf.
3
Analyse each position's dalil
Mainstream: outward contract and possession create guarantee. Abu Yusuf: their agreement negates guarantee; satisfaction (istifa') requires a debt.
Both positions are aqli (rational) arguments.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: if the pawn perishes, under the mainstream view the pledgee must pay its value; under Abu Yusuf, no compensation is due.
The difference affects financial liability.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The text indicates that the mainstream position (as in the matn) is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) according to zahir al-riwayah.
Fatwa follows the guarantee position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وببدل صلح عن إنكار إن أقر أن لا دين، صالح مع إنكار ورهن ببدل الصلح شيئا. (٣)قوله : صالح مع إنكار ... الخ؛ توضيح هذه الصورة: أن رجلاً ادعى ألف درهم فجحد المدعى عليه، ثم صالحه من ذلك على خمسمئة على الإنكار، وأعطاه بها رهناً يساوي خمسمئة، فهلك الرهن عند المرتهن ثم تصادقا على أن لا دين، فالرهن مضمون كما ذكر. [١]قوله : فالرهن مضمون ; وفي «الهداية»: وعن أبي يوسف الله خلافه... الخ. وفي «العناية»: لأنهما لما تصادقا أن لا دين، فقد تصادقا على عدم الضمان، وتصادقهما حجّة في حقهما، والاستيفاء بدون الدين لا يتصوّر.
And with a compromise amount on denial if he admits there is no debt: he made a compromise with denial and pawned something with the compromise amount. (3) His saying: compromise with denial... etc.; clarification of this scenario: a man claimed a thousand dirhams and the defendant denied, then he compromised with him for five hundred on the basis of denial, and gave him a pawn equal to five hundred as security; the pawn perished with the pledgee; then both agreed that there was no debt; so the pawn is guaranteed as mentioned. Footnote [1]: His saying: so the pawn is guaranteed; and in Al-Hidayah: and from Abu Yusuf there is a difference... etc. And in Al-'Inayah: because when they both agreed that there is no debt, they agreed on the absence of guarantee, and their agreement is a proof against them, and satisfaction without debt is inconceivable.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage presents a scenario of pawning with a compromise amount (badal sulh) on denial of debt. The scenario: a claimant demands 1000 dirhams from a defendant who denies liability. They settle on 500 dirhams as a compromise on denial (sulh 'ala al-inkar), and the defendant gives the claimant a pawn worth 500 dirhams. The pawn perishes with the pledgee. Later, both parties admit that there was actually no debt. The ruling is that the pawn is still guaranteed (madhmun) – the pledgee must compensate the pledgor. The reasoning is that the outward contract and possession of the pawn create guarantee, even if the underlying debt is later negated. The text then notes an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: from Abu Yusuf, there is a different opinion. Al-'Inayah explains that if both agree there was no debt, they effectively agree there was no guarantee, and their agreement is binding; without a debt, satisfaction (istifa') by retention of the pawn is not valid. The relied-upon position (zahir al-riwayah) appears to be that the pawn is guaranteed, as stated in the main text.

Key Terms
بدل صلح (badal sulh): The amount paid in a compromise settlement; here given as security for a compromise on denial.
صلح عن إنكار (sulh 'ala al-inkar): A compromise made when the defendant denies the claim; the claimant accepts less to avoid dispute.
غرم (ghurm): Guarantee/liability; here the pawnbroker is liable for the pawn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The pawn is returned if intact, but if lost, the pledgee pays half.

Key Takeaway
The outward validity of a pawn contract creates a guarantee that is not undone by a later mutual admission that no debt existed.

Compensating a Lost Pawn of the Same Type: By Weight or Value?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on compensation for pawn of gold/silver and mawzun/makil when lost

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on compensating a lost pawn of the same type and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
When a pawn of the same type as the debt perishes, Abu Hanifa requires compensation by weight only; the Sahibayn require market value.

When a pawned item is of the same type as the debt (e.g., silver for a debt of silver), its loss raises a critical question: should the pawnbroker compensate with the same weight of silver, or with the market value? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions gave different answers, each based on a distinct analysis of the legal nature of the loss. This lesson unpacks their reasoning and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the ruling
Identify the exact factual situation: a pawn of the same genus as the debt (e.g., a silver ewer for a debt of silver) is lost. Abu Hanifa's ruling is compensation by like in quantity (weight/measure) without considering quality.
The debt is of the same type, so paying back by weight is like returning exactly what was owed.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the pawnbroker must pay the market value of the lost item, not its weight. They argue that weight-only compensation could harm the pawnbroker if the item's value is much higher.
This is a valid ikhtilaf within the school.
3
Analyze the core legal reasoning
Abu Hanifa views the loss as 'istihlak' (consumption) where satisfaction (istifa') is by weight; the Sahibayn view the loss as a 'satisfaction event' but apply the principle of preventing harm (darar).
The dispute hinges on whether the state of destruction is analogous to consumption or to satisfaction with risk of harm.
4
Work through the example
Apply the scenario: a silver ewer weighing 10 dirhams pawned for 10 dirhams, lost. Under Abu Hanifa: pawnbroker pays 10 dirhams weight of silver. Under Sahibayn: pays the market value (e.g., 500 dirhams).
This illustrates the dramatic thamarah al-khilaf – practical consequence.
5
Evaluate the mufta bihi
Although Abu Hanifa's view is the zahir al-riwayah, later fatwa often adopts the Sahibayn's view to avoid harm. Know which position is operative in your context.
This reflects the Hanafi tradition's concern for public welfare (maslahah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورهن الحجرين والمكيل والموزون، فإن رهن بجنسه فهلكه بمثلِهِ قدراً من دينه، ولا عبرة للجودة. قوله : قدراً ; تميز من مثله ; أي يعتبر المماثلة في القدر، وهو الوزن والكيل بلا اعتبار الجودة، وعندهما : يعتبر القيمة فيقوم بخلاف الجنس، ويكون رهنا مكانه، فإن رهن إبريق فضَّةٍ وزنُهُ عشرة دراهم بعشرة دراهم، فهلك على المرتهن قيمة الرهن خمسمئة للراهن في ظاهر الرواية. [٢] قوله : ورهن الحجرين ; أي يجوز رهن الذهب والفضة لما عرفت أن كل ما يمكن الاستيفاء منه جاز أن يرهن بدين مضمون، والذهب والفضة على هذه الصفة، فيجوز رهنهما. [٣] قوله : أي يعتبر المماثلة... الخ ; فلا يعتبر بالجودة عند المقابلة بجنسها، وهذا أبي حنيفة ; لأنَّ عنده يصيرُ مستوفياً باعتبار الوزن دون القيمة. [٤] قوله : ويكون رهنا ... الخ ; لأنه لو صار مستوفياً يتضرر المرتهن، فالأصلُ عنده أن حالة الهلاك حالة الاستهلاك، والاستيفاء إنّما يكون بالوزن، وعندهما : حالة الهلاكِ حالة الاستيفاء إذا لم يفض إلى الضرر. «كفاية». [0] قوله : إبريق : كوزه بالوله با دسته و این معرب ابريزست، أباريق جمع. كذا في «منتخب اللغات».
And the pawning of the two stones (gold and silver) and measured and weighed items; if he pawns with its own type, then its loss is with its like in quantity from his debt, and no consideration for quality. His saying: 'in quantity' distinguishes from 'its like'; meaning similarity in quantity is considered, which is weight and measure without considering quality. And according to them (the two companions), value is considered, so it is evaluated contrary to the type, and it becomes a pawn in its place. For example, if he pawns a silver ewer weighing ten dirhams for ten dirhams, and it perishes, then upon the pawnbroker is the value of the pawn, five hundred [dirhams] to the pledgor according to the apparent narration. Footnote [2]: His saying: and pawning the two stones – meaning pawning gold and silver is permissible because you already know that anything from which satisfaction can be taken is permissible to pawn for a guaranteed debt, and gold and silver are of this description, so pawning them is permissible. Footnote [3]: His saying: meaning similarity is considered... etc. – so quality is not considered when matching with its type, and this is the opinion of Abu Hanifa, because according to him he becomes satisfied by weight without value. Footnote [4]: His saying: and it becomes a pawn... etc. – because if he were to become satisfied, the pawnbroker would be harmed; so the principle with him is that the state of destruction is a state of consumption, and satisfaction is only by weight; while according to them (the two companions), the state of destruction is a state of satisfaction if it does not lead to harm. From Kifayah. Footnote [0]: His saying: ewer – a pitcher with a handle and spout; it is an Arabization of [Persian] 'ābrīz'; its plural is 'abārīq'. From Muntakhab al-Lughat.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on pawning gold, silver, and items sold by weight (mawzūn) or measure (makīl). If a person pawns an item of the same type as the debt (e.g., gold for a debt of gold), and the pawn perishes, then the loss is compensated by the like in quantity (weight or measure) without regard to quality. So if a silver ewer weighing 10 dirhams is pawned for a debt of 10 dirhams, and it is lost, the pawnbroker owes 10 dirhams in weight (not the ewer's market value, which might be 500 dirhams). This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa. However, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that the pawnbroker must pay the value of the pawn (qīmah), not the like in weight. The reason for Abu Hanifa's view: when the pawn is of the same type as the debt, losing it is considered as if the debtor has consumed (consumed) the pawn, and satisfaction (istīfā') of the debt is achieved by weight/measure. Since the debt is of the same type, paying back in like in quantity is sufficient. The Sahibayn argue that this would harm the pawnbroker because the value may be much higher than the weight, so they require payment of value to prevent harm. The text also notes that pawning gold and silver is permissible because they are properties from which satisfaction can be taken. The last footnote is a lexical note explaining 'ibrīq' (ewer).

Key Terms
الحجرين (al-hajarayn): The two stones: metaphor for gold and silver (because they are mined).
المماثلة في القدر (al-mumathala fi al-qadr): Equality in quantity (weight or measure), not quality.
استيفاء (istifa'): Satisfaction of the debt by receiving the like in type and quantity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a silver ewer weighing 10 dirhams is pawned for a debt of 10 dirhams and is lost, how much must the pawnbroker pay?
A) The market value of the ewer (e.g., 500 dirhams).
B) 10 dirhams in weight of silver.
C) The ewer itself cannot be pawned because it is silver.
D) Nothing, because the pawn was of the same type.

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute on compensating a lost pawn of the same type, Abu Hanifa requires weight/measure; the Sahibayn require value – the operative ruling often follows the latter to prevent harm.

Pawning Gold and Silver: The Perishing Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on perishing of same-type pledges

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on pawning same-type items (gold, silver, mithli), state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to a concrete scenario.

Core Idea
When a pledge of the same type perishes, quality is irrelevant: Abu Hanifa holds debt satisfied by weight, Sahibayn require value-based replacement.

When a pawned item is of the same type as the debt (e.g., gold for gold), and it perishes, how is the debt affected? This lesson explores the Hanafi stance and an intra-madhhab disagreement. We will examine the proof of Abu Hanifa, who considers the pledge consumed by weight, and the proof of the two disciples (Sahibayn), who fear harm to the mortgagee and thus require a replacement pledge. The practical difference is crucial for pawnbrokers and creditors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
The matn rules that pawning gold, silver, and measured/weighed items (mithli) with a debt of the same type is permissible. Understanding the basic permissibility is the foundation.
All Hanafi imams agree on permissibility.
2
Formulate the dispute on perishing
The dispute arises when the pledge perishes: does the debt become satisfied by the weight of the pledge (Abu Hanifa) or does the mortgagee need to purchase a replacement based on the pledge's value (Sahibayn)?
The core disagreement is over the mechanism of satisfaction (istifa').
3
Analyse Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa argues that quality is irrelevant in ribawi items of the same kind; receiving good for bad is permissible in sarf and salam, so the pledge is simply 'consumed' by the debt by weight. It is impossible to break the transaction because one cannot guarantee one's own property.
His dalil relies on istihsan and consensus (ijma) on permissible exchange of quality in same-type ribawi transactions.
4
Analyse the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn argue that receiving by weight alone could harm the mortgagee if the pledge is of higher quality than its weight indicates, and considering value leads to riba. So they resort to making the pledge a guarantee (tadmin) that breaks possession and requires a replacement.
Their approach avoids both harm and riba by modifying the transaction.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Apply the dispute to a concrete case: a gold cup weighing 10 dirhams pawned for a 10-dirham debt perishes. Under Abu Hanifa, the debt is fully cleared. Under the Sahibayn, if the cup's market value is only 8 dirhams, the mortgagee must purchase 8 dirhams of gold as a new pledge; the debt remains partially unsatisfied.
The difference affects the creditor's risk and the debtor's liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورهن الحجرين والمكيل والموزون ، فإن رهن بجنسه فهلكه بمثله قدراً من دينه ، ولا عبرة للجودة . ثم تصادقا على أن لا دين ، فالرهن مضمون كما ذكر. قوله : ورهن الحجرين؛ أي يجوز رهن الذهب والفضة لما عرفت أن كل ما يمكن الاستيفاء منه جاز أن يرهن بدين مضمون، والذهب والفضة على هذه الصفة، فيجوز رهنهما. قوله : أي يعتبر المماثلة في القدر دون الجودة عند المقابلة بجنسها، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله؛ لأن عنده يصير مستوفياً باعتبار الوزن دون القيمة. فعند أبي حنيفة هلك بالدين، وعندهما إن كان قيمته مثل وزنه أو أكثر فكذا، وإن كان قيمته أقل وهي ثمانية مثلاً يشتري بثمانية دراهم ذهب ليكون رهناً مكانه. دليله أن الجودة ساقطة العبرة في الأموال الربوية عند المقابلة بجنسها، واستيفاء الجيد بالرديء جائز، كما إذا تجوز به في بدل الصرف والسلم، وقد حصل الاستيفاء بالإجماع؛ ولهذا يحتاج إلى نقضه، ولا يمكن نقضه بإيجاب الضمان؛ لأنه لا بد له من مطالب ومطالب، وكذا الإنسان لا يضمن ملك نفسه، ويتعذر التضمين بتعذر النقض. كذا في «الهداية». دليلهما أنه لا وجه إلى الاستيفاء بالوزن لما فيه من الضرر بالمرتهن، ولا وجه إلى اعتبار القيمة؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى الربا، فصرنا إلى التضمين بخلاف الجنس لينتقض القبض، ويجعل مكانه، ثم يتملكه. فإن قيل: في هذا التركيب، وهو قوله: فهلكه بمثله قدراً من دينه؛ نظر لأن الدين إذا كان خمسة عشر ووزنه عشرة وقد هلك، فقد هلك بعشرة دراهم من الدين، فعلى المديون خمسة، فيكون من للتبعيض، فلا يتناول ما إذا كان وزنه عشرة والدين عشرة؛ لأن التبعيض غير ممكن، ولا يكون للبيان هنا؛ لأنه لما ثم بيانه: إن لا بد من كون لفظة: من في قوله: من دينه؛ للتبعيض؛ لأن الدين إذا كان زائداً، ووزن الرهن الهالك أقل منه، كما إذا كان الدين خمسة عشرة والوزن عشرة، فإذا هلك ما وزنه عشرة، فقد هلك من الدين ما ...
And the pledge of the two stones (gold and silver) and the measured and weighed: if he pledges by its like, then its destruction counts as a like amount from his debt, and quality is not considered. Then if they affirm that there is no debt, the pledge is guaranteed as mentioned. His statement: 'and the pledge of the two stones' means it is permissible to pledge gold and silver because you know that whatever can be satisfied from it is permissible to be pledged for a guaranteed debt, and gold and silver are so, so pledging them is permissible. His statement: 'similarity is considered in quantity not quality when matched with its kind, and this is according to Abu Hanifa, because according to him one becomes recipient by weight not value. So according to Abu Hanifa, it perishes against the debt; according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if its value is like its weight or more, then likewise; but if its value is less – for example eight – he purchases with eight dirhams of gold to be a pledge in its place. His proof: that quality is disregarded in ribawi wealth when matched with its kind, and receiving good for bad is permissible, as when it is done in the exchange of gold/silver (sarf) and salam, and receiving is achieved by consensus; hence it requires breaking (the transaction), and it cannot be broken by imposing guarantee because he has a claimant, and likewise a person does not guarantee his own property, and guarantee becomes impossible due to impossibility of breaking. So in Al-Hidayah. Their proof: that there is no way to receive by weight due to harm to the mortgagee, and no way to consider value because it leads to riba; so we resort to guarantee unlike its kind so that the possession is broken and it is placed in its place, then he takes ownership. If it is said: in this construction, his saying: 'its destruction as a like amount from his debt' – consider, because if the debt is fifteen and its weight is ten and it perishes, then it perishes against ten dirhams of the debt, so on the debtor five; thus 'min' is for partiality, so it does not include the case where its weight is ten and the debt is ten because partiality is impossible, and it is not for explanation here because... then its explanation: that the word 'min' in his saying 'from his debt' must be for partiality, because if the debt is greater and the weight of the perished pledge is less, like if the debt is fifteen and the weight ten, so when what weighs ten perishes, it has perished from the debt...
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the fate of a pawned item (rahn) when it perishes, specifically when the pawned item is of the same type as the debt (e.g., gold for gold). The matn states the general ruling: if one pawns gold, silver, or measured/weighed items (mithliyyat) as a pledge for a debt of the same type, and the pledge perishes, then the debt is considered satisfied by the weight of the pledge, regardless of quality. The commentary then explains that this ruling is according to Abu Hanifa, who considers that the pledge is 'consumed' by the debt based on weight, not value. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: they require consideration of value, so if the pledge's value is less than its weight, they require purchasing a replacement pledge of equivalent value. The text then provides proofs for each position: Abu Hanifa relies on the principle that in ribawi transactions of the same type, quality is irrelevant, and receiving bad for good is allowed (as in sarf and salam), so the pledge is simply considered received by weight; it would be impossible to break the transaction and impose a guarantee. The Sahibayn argue that receiving by weight would harm the mortgagee (since the pledge might be of higher quality), and considering value would lead to riba; so they resort to treating the pledge as a guarantee that breaks possession and requires a replacement. The passage also addresses a grammatical objection about the phrase 'from his debt' (min daini), explaining that the preposition 'min' here indicates partiality, appropriate when the debt exceeds the pledge weight, but troublesome when they are equal; the response clarifies that the ruling covers both cases, and the 'min' is indeed for partiality.

Key Terms
الحجران (al-hajarayn): Refers to gold and silver, considered ribawi items in Hanafi fiqh.
المثل (al-mithl): In the context of pledge, refers to a thing of the same type and quantity (like weight) but not necessarily same quality.
الاستيفاء (al-istifa'): Satisfaction of a debt by receiving its equivalent; here, the creditor's receipt of the pledge in satisfaction of the debt.
التضمين (al-tadmin): Making the pledge a guarantee or liability; holding the pledge as security that must be compensated if it perishes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is permitted and similar items are treated without considering quality upon perishing.
B) It is only permitted if the debt is less than the weight of the pledge.
C) It is not permitted because of riba.
D) It is permitted but quality must be exactly matched.

Key Takeaway
When a pledge of the same type and species perishes, the Hanafi madhhab's relied-upon view is that the debt is satisfied by the weight of the pledge, regardless of quality or market value.

Destruction of the Pawn: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the ruling and proof for destroyed pawn, followed by linguistic analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi dispute on the destruction of a pawn and explain the linguistic proof (min as partitive) that supports Abu Hanifa's position.

Core Idea
When a pawn is destroyed, Abu Hanifa counts it by weight against the debt; the Sahibayn require replacement if value is less.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement on the legal consequence when a pawned item (rahn) is destroyed while in the pledgee's possession. The dispute turns on whether the destruction is treated as satisfaction of the debt by weight or whether the pledgee must guarantee the pawn. The argument is supported by a detailed linguistic analysis of the preposition 'min' in the phrase 'min daynihi'.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa and that of the two companions (Sahibayn) regarding the destruction of a pawn.
Abu Hanifa: halaka bi al-dayn (destroyed by the debt, by weight). Sahibayn: if value < weight, buy replacement of same weight.
2
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Examine the evidence: quality is disregarded in ribawi goods, taking good for bad is permissible in sarf/salam, possession is established by consensus and cannot be undone without demand, and one cannot guarantee one's own property.
The proof rests on the principle that possession once validly taken remains in effect.
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
Understand their objection: taking by weight harms the pledgee, considering value leads to riba, so they adopt a guarantee (tadmeen) to protect the pledgee.
They prioritize preventing harm to the pledgee and avoiding riba.
4
Examine the linguistic objection
The objection argues that 'min' in 'min daynihi' must be partitive, which would not cover cases where weight equals or exceeds the debt, causing a deficiency in the expression.
The objection claims that if 'min' were for clarification (bayan), it would lead to the problem that a homonym (mushtarak) cannot have general application.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The answer clarifies that 'min' is exclusively partitive, and the statement is only meant to illustrate the principle of considering weight, not to cover every possible scenario; the purpose is to show that destruction is by weight, not value.
The objection is dismissed as irrelevant to the core legal point.

So if it is intended that it be for clarification to include this case, then it is objected: it has become necessary to interpret it as partition in one case, and if it is for clarification in another case, then it necessitates the general application of a homonym, although it is established in usul al-fiqh that a homonym has no general application. And even if this prohibition is violated, there is no escape from the objection. For then it does not include the case where its weight is greater and the debt less, e.g. weight fifteen and debt ten, due to the impossibility of both partition and clarification; for it would then mean that its destruction is by the amount of fifteen from a total of ten dirhams, which is as you see. Summary of the answer: 'min' is for partition, not for clarification, because the purpose is to indicate that the destruction is considered by weight not value, and this much is apparent from the expression in the case of partition. As for the expression not including the case of equality or the case where weight exceeds the debt, this is not harmful because the aim is only to depict the purpose, not more. So reflect. [1] His statement: 'because the homonym has no general application... etc.'; know that the homonym is that which applies to different individuals with different definitions in an alternative manner, like 'man' in our discussion, for it is shared between the meaning of partition and clarification, and it has no general application, i.e. for the homonym according to us, because the coinage assigned the word to a meaning such that it is not intended.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling when a pawned item (rahn) is destroyed while in the possession of the pledgee (murtahin). The passage begins by stating the positions: According to Abu Hanifa, the destruction of the pawn is considered against the debt (halaka bi al-dayn) — meaning that the pawn is regarded as having been taken in satisfaction of an equal weight of the debt, so the debtor still owes the balance. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), if the value of the pawn equals or exceeds its weight, the same applies; but if its value is less, the pledgee must purchase a replacement of the same weight (here with gold) so that the pawn remains intact. The text then provides the proofs: Abu Hanifa's argument is that quality is disregarded in ribawi goods when exchanging like-for-like; taking good for bad is permissible as in sarf and salam; and since possession has already occurred by consensus, undoing it would require a demand and a demander, but one cannot guarantee one's own property. The two companions argue that taking possession by weight would harm the pledgee, and considering value leads to riba, so they adopt a guarantee (tadmeen) approach, unlike the genus where possession is undone and replaced. The passage then raises a linguistic objection: the phrase 'min daynihi' (from his debt) in the statement 'halaka bi mithlihi qadran min daynihi' must be interpreted as partitive (li al-tab'id) because the debt exceeds the weight. If it were for clarification (li al-bayan), it would lead to the problem of a homonym (mushtarak) having general application, which is invalid in Hanafi usul. The answer clarifies that 'min' is exclusively partitive, and the purpose of the statement is only to show that destruction is considered by weight, not by value; the expression is not intended to cover every case (such as equality or excess weight), so the objection is dismissed. This passage demonstrates the interplay between fiqh reasoning and linguistic analysis in classical Hanafi scholarship.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A pledge or pawn – a valuable item handed over as security for a debt.
هلك (halaka): Destroyed – the pawn perishes while in the pledgee's custody.
من (min): The Arabic preposition used in 'min daynihi' – argued to be partitive (li al-tab'id) in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) By its value
B) By its weight
C) Not considered at all
D) Only if the debtor agrees

Key Takeaway
The destruction of a pawn is treated as satisfaction of the debt by weight according to Abu Hanifa, supported by linguistic analysis of 'min', while the Sahibayn require replacement if value is less.

Sale Conditioned on a Pledge or Guarantor: Istihsan Over Qiyas
Ruling on a sale conditioned on a pledge or guarantor, with istihsan reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a sale conditioned on a specific pledge or guarantor, explain the istihsan reasoning, and apply the conditions for its validity.

Core Idea
A sale with a pledge condition is valid by istihsan because the condition is suitable to secure the debt.

This passage addresses a common transaction scenario: a buyer agrees to provide a specific pledge or guarantor as part of the sale, but later refuses. While strict analogy (qiyas) would invalidate such a sale as a prohibited 'double transaction', the Hanafi school upholds its validity through istihsan (juristic preference). The key is that the condition is harmonious with the contract's purpose of securing payment. In this lesson, we unpack the reasoning, the necessary conditions, and the practical consequences when the buyer fails to deliver.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its apparent ruling
The act is a sale where the buyer conditions the purchase on giving a specified pledge or specified guarantor. At first glance, qiyas would rule it invalid because it resembles 'safqah fi safqah' (one transaction within another).
The qiyas ruling is the default by analogy to other forbidden conditions.
2
Understand the istihsan reasoning
The Hanafi school overrides qiyas by istihsan. The condition of a pledge or guarantor is 'mula'im' (suitable/appropriate) because it serves to secure the obligation (al-istithiq), which is harmonious with the contract's purpose. Thus it does not corrupt the sale.
Istihsan is a source of law in Hanafi usul.
3
Apply the conditions for validity
The pledge must be specified (mu'ayyan) and the guarantor must be present in the session (majlis) or accept during the session. If the pledge is unspecified or the guarantor is absent and not accepted, the contract is invalid because the condition loses its suitability.
This is derived from the commentary in the passage.
4
Analyze the intra-madhhab dispute on compulsion
If the buyer refuses to deliver the specified pledge, the Hanafi majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold that the buyer cannot be compelled because the pledge is a gratuitous contract (tabarru'). Zufar argues compulsion. The relied-upon position is no compulsion, but the seller has the option to rescind the sale.
The seller's option arises because the condition was an incentive for the sale.
5
State the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference between the Hanafi view and Zufar's view is whether the buyer can be forced to hand over the pledge. In the Hanafi view, the seller must either accept without the pledge or cancel the sale. If the buyer pays the price immediately (hālan), the seller's option lapses.
This is an important nuance for real transactions.

Source Text
